This report is based on the findings of preappraisal and appraisal missions
to Chile on March 4 to 27, and June 24 to July 10, 1991.  The missions were
composed by Mr. Raul Auzmendi, Sr. Economist (Mission Leader), Mr. Gustavo Unda,
Sr. Port Engineer, Ms. Marta Rios, Sr. Financial Analyst and Me. Marta Molares,
Sr. Counsel. The report was prepared under the general direction of Mr. Ping-
Cheung Loh, Director LA4DR and Mr. Alain Thys, Chief LA4IE. Mr. Robert Panfil,
LA2IE, acted as the Peer Reviewer.

I =  IRMNSfOLT  ?CTOR
A. Ovanjgr
1.01      Thg AconqW.  In the mid 1980s, the Chiloan economy, coming out of a
deep recession, underwent a Bank-supported structural adjustment program, which
had as an objective the recovery of economic activity and employment, coupled
with  gradual  reduction  of  the  external  debt  burden.    The  successful
implementation of the program and falling world oil prices, led to the expansion
of the economy in 1986 and 1987, with GDP growing by 5.72 in both years.
Overall, in the five-yaar period 1984-88, the Chilean economy averaged a growth
rate of 5.52, infl*tion was reduced from 232 to 132 and unemployment declined
sharply, reaching 6.62 of the labor force by the end of 1988.  During 1989,
growth accoleratod to 102 and the official unemployment rate declined further to
52.  However, the further acceleration in economic activity exacted a cost.
Inflation reversed its 1988 downward trend, with consumer pricea accelerating
from an an.aual 12.71 in December 1988 to 21.4Z at yoar-end 1989.
1.02      During 1990, Chile underwent another signzficant adjustment effort
aimed at addressing the fiscal Imbalance that had emerged in 1989 owing to the
overheating of the economy, and the jump in international petroleum prices during
the second half of 1990. In addition, there were significant changes in economic
management: (a) a new administration was sworn-in in March 1990 with a new
economic  team;  and  (b)  the  Central  Bank  became  autonomous.    The  new
Administration has underscored its intent to maintain the ongoing overall
economic policy framework, i.oe, endorsing the concept of an open, market-
oriented economy.  In this contoxt, economic results, and more importantly,
overall economic management in 1990, were broadly satisfactory. The adjustment
effort resulted in a fall in GDP growth from 102 in 1989 to 2.12 in 1990.
Although inflation increased to 27.32 in 1990, international reserves nearly
doubled and inve3tment rmained satisfactory. Economic growth is expected to
accelerate in 1991 to an annual rate of between 42 and 5S while inflation would
fall to around 202. At the same time, the fiscal budget is projected to be
balanced.  The pick-up in economic growth in 1991 will be accompanied by an
increase in transport demand. For the medium term, economic growth in Chile is
projected to remain strong at an average annual GDP rate of 4.52.
1.03      Transoort Scope and Demand.  Chile covers an area of about 757,000
km2.  It comprises a narrow strip of land between the Andes and the Pacific
Ocean, extending some 4,300 km in the direction N-S and its width varies from a
minimum of 90 km to a maximum of 400 km. The population was estimated at 13
million in 1989, about 702 of whom live in a narrow area about 600 km long
(between the rivers Aconcagua and Bio-Bio) and the country's widt'. in this
stretch. In 1987, the urtan population represented 85S of the total population,
one of the highest in the world. Chile's population is expected to grow in the
next decade at an annual rate of 1.52, and the urban population at 1.732 per
year. Chile is located far from the world's main traffic generators.
1.04      Transport demand has kept pace with economic development in the
country. During the period 1985-90, the general trend has been a growth of about
3.5-42 per year for freight and 52 per year for passengers.  In line with
worldwide trends, and despite the country's shape and proximity to ths sea, the
transport system is dominated by road transport, which carries about two-thirds
of domestic freight, railways hauling around one-fifth, coaetal shipping and oil



pipeline. combined carrying about one-tenth, and air transport handling less than
1S. Some 95Z of international trade in tone is seaborne (some 90S of exports and
abo.t 801 of imports by value). Passenger traffic is carried mainly by bus,
although there is some rail service.
1.05      To meet the transport demand, the country has developed an extensive
transport network (Map IERD 23102) consisting of 79,000.km of roads, of which
almo&. 14Z are pavoi; 6,900 km of railway t:'ack, about 581 of which is broad
gauge (1.676 metor), and the rest is narrow gauge (one moter); eleven major
general cargo, state-owned seaports and a large number of specialized and private
general cargo portst a 622 km network of pipelines; and about 21 commercial
airport., of which seven are used for international flights. The transport system
is operated by a large, priAately-owned fleet of trucks and buses; a publicly-
owned rseilwayl a mix of oublic and private oceangoing and coastal shipping
companies; and a number of privately-owned airlines of which three provide
international service.
1.06      With more than 5,000 km of coastline, Chile has more than 70 port
installations of which 26 are commercial ports involved in international traffic.
In 1990, these ports handled some 28.6 million tons of international traffic, 39Z
of which was handled by the eleven publicly-owned ports under the jurisdiction
of the Chilean Ports Enterprise (EMPORCHI) and the balance by 15 private berths
and piers (Tables 1.1 and 1.2). The trend of international traffic in the 1980.
has been rather flat at around 17 million tons until 1985, followed by a vigorous
increase of about 14.5Z p.a. onwards (Chart 1). National coastal traffic showed
a similar trend during the 1980s, reaching about 9.2 million tons in 1989.
During the same period, container traffic had a significant increase of almost
fivefold, reaching in 1989 a value of over 2.1 million tons in about 177,000
containers (124,000 loaded and 53,000 empties). Container traffic is moved mainly
through the EMPORCHI ports, of which Valparaiso accounts for about 601. Traffic
is expected to continue growing at a high rate in the foreseeable future.  A
description of each transport mode is provided in Annex 1 of this report, and
Annex 2 presents expected port traffic forecasts.
1.07      Transport policymaking in Chile is officially the task of the Ministry
of Transport and Telecommunications (MINTRATEL); the dinistry of Public Works
(MOP) does road planning and is in charge of all transport infrastructure works,
except urban.    The  Ministry  of  Housing  and  Urbanization  (MINVU)  bears
responsibility for the urban road network nation-wide except for a central
section in the city of Santiago.  The Ministry of Planning and Coordination
(MIDEPLAN) updates a rolling three-year plan every year, and determines who-her
proposed transport investments are justified according to its guidelines as well
as whether ttkey fit into the broader planning framework.
1.08      The Government agency responsible for public port rehabilitation and
construction is the Directorate of Port Works (DOP) under the MOP.   This
directorato is an efficient and experienced agency, with a wall defined structure
and is adequately staffed to fulfill the functions it is normally responsible
for. However, it will require external support through consulting services to
cope with oncoming increases in its workload.  DOP is headed by a national
director, assisted by an advisory group, and has four departments (studies,
planning and coordination, construction, and equipment) and one administrative



subdepartment in the central office in Santiago. This set-up is complemented by
eight Regional Directorates directly respunoible for all field work. Details of
the organization are shown in Chart 2. The total staff consists of 123 employees
as follows: one National Director, nine managers, forty-eight professionals,
twonty-five technicians, and forty administrative clerks,
B. Trgnstort Sector Reform of the 1980s
1.09      'he traneport osctor underwent considerable restructuring in line with
the strongly market-oriented transport strategy pursued by the count%y in the
last decade. Policy cha.gSes included: (a) eliminating transport sector subsidies;
(b) charging private air, land, and sea transport for the use of public
infrastructure facilities; (c) permitting each transport mode to set its own
tariffs, design its services, And develop its own policies; (d) liberalizing
access to the road transport industry; (a) encouraging privato sector
participation for investments and operations in ports, airlines, railways and
urban transport; and (f) considering all transport modes equal for purposes of
taxation. As a recult of these changes, the country has developed an adequate
intermodal framework to achieve optimum resource allocation.
1.10      In part4cular, in the 1980s the Chilean ports system underwent a major
institutional restructuring based primarily on transferring to the private sector
the operation of public ports, with the exception of wharfage and stor&3e. This
significantly improved public port efficiency during the 1981-89 period,
achieving increases ranging from 1002 to 2002 in the number of tons
loaded/unloaded of the main products per berthed ship-day, with port cost savings
estimated at about US$90 million in 1989, as compared to what would have been
required in 1981 for moving the same volume. As a result, in the 1980s, Chilean
ports were able to maintain reasonable operating conditions even with
considerable growth in the volume of cargo and without an increase in
infrastructure, some of which was destroyed or seriously damaged by the 1985
earthquake. But the institutional improvements and technological changes now have
reached a limit where extra port capacity in the system is badly needed.
1.11      The modernization of Chile's ports was based on Law No. 18,032 and
18,042, borh enacted in late 1981.  Also, Lad No. 18,040 of December 1981
modified the Customs Ordinance to simplify port procedures, contributing to a
general improvement in the subsector. Law 18,032 specifically abolished the
system of registration with maritime workers' unions: any worker who met the
minimum requirements of physical fitness was authorized to work in the ports on
board vessels and on the docks. Such people were issued a Port Worker's Permit.
Under this law, workers could establiah trade unions as well as professional
associations, but only the company un4_ s were authorized to hold negotiations.
To make these changes acceptable to the port workers, the Government paid the
equivalent of around US$40 million to compensate about 2,700 workers whose
privileges were foregone. Also in 1981, two resolutions were passed (Roes. 12600
and 360), which established three 7.5-hour shifts for all workers in all ports
of the nation. Hence, po-ts could be operational for 22.5 hours a day instead
of 13 hours, as was the case before. The first article of Law 18,042 abolished
the monopoly that the public enterprise, EMPORCHI, had enjoyed over dock work
(i.e., the area between crane hook and warehouse) and authorized the private
sector to carry out such work. The other articles of that law established the



- 4 -
basis for creating a holdiaig-type National Ports Corporation that would own
Limited Liability Authorities (Sociesdads Anonimas) to be created in each of the
11 ports under EMPORCHI. An Interminittrial Commiasion was created to deal with
the implementation of those articles; however, the commission could not arrive
at a consensus, so the application of Law 18,042, with the exception of the first
article, was suspended.  Finally, Law No. 18,966 of 1990 abolished Law No.
18,042, establishing that the sector would continue to be ruled by the original
Law that created EMPORCHI (No. 290 of 1960), modified, however, to es;clude
EMPORCHI from stevedoring and nargo handling functions, which would remain in
private hands.
1.12      There are several agencies involved in the planning process for port
infrastructure.    Their  actions  are  coordinated  by  an  Interministerial
Infrastructure Committee (IIC), presided over by the Minister of Public Works5
in addition,  tLere is an executive Interministerial Port Commi-n:ion (IPC)
responsible for operational and technical matters of port projects.   This
Commission, however, plays an adv ory role and depends on existing agencies to
carry out its functions. This interministerial set-up provides an adequate forum
within which to discuss and reach consensus on major decisions in the sector.
The private sector does not formally participate in the port planning process,
although it influences it, especially during the last decade, through the
evaluation and execution of specific private port investments.  The Government
encourages maximum private participation for expansion of port capacity,
purposely limiting its own participation to rehabilitation and maintenance.
State investments for new construction are made only when private initiative does
not materialize or for national security reasons.
1.13      As a result of these reforms, the role of the private sector has been
and will continue to be very important for the development and operation of the
port system. The 15 privately owned ports consist of termLnahl constructed by
mining or oil companies or by other private interests and handle about 602 of the
country's foreign trade measured in tons. However, in value terms its share is
lower, because EMPORCHI handles almost 901 of all general cargo in the country,
even though non-EMPORCHI ports are also free to handle third-party traffic. But,
except in the Concepcion area, where the private port of Lirquen is growing
rapidly, the scope for handling third-party traffic by private ports is limited.
Moreover, most of the private port facilitios are sopecialized terminals handling
low- to medium-value bulk cargo.
1.14      In sum, as a result of these reforms, all EMPORCHI ports are operated
by private stevedoring companies um.der concession contracts, except for wharfage
and storage, which remain under EMPORC!T's responsibility All cargo handling
equipment as well as one of the gantry cranes in Valparaiso are provided by
private operators. The rest of the gantry cranes are owned by EMPORCHI, but have
been transferred to private operators under lease agreements. RMPORCHI, which
retains ownersh;.p over port infrastructure, is also responsible for cooruinating
the activities of the vario s users of the port area.
1.15      EMPORCHI is a well managed organization, with a suitable mix of staff,
and adequately fulfills the role it is expected to play. EMPORCHI's headquarters
are located in Valparaiso, with local offices at each of its ports. It is headed
by a national director appointed by the President of the Republic. The director



- 5 -
is re3ponsible for, inter alla, executing the Government's public ports policy,
coordinating the activities of the ports with those of other port-related
transport sectors,  rehabilitating and maintaining port  infrastructure,  and
establishing and efficiently managing the port authority so it can fulfill its
duties.  Under the entity's present organization (Chart 3), the director is
asaisted by five advisory departments (planning, controllers, legal, systems, and
secretary) and by six line departments responsible for the functions of
markot±ng, engineering, special projects, financ-, perscnnel, and projects and
contracts.  Each EMPORCHI port has its own director, also ropor.ing to the
national director, who is responsible for operational, technical, financial, and
personnel functions at the local level. In particular, maintenance of EMPORCHI
port facilities is adequately carried out and sufficient resources are allocated
every year for that purpose.   Although INPORCHI  is an autonomous public
enterprise and receives no budgetary support from the Government, in managing its
finances it must abide by the rules and regulation. imposeod by the Finance
Ministry to all autonomous public enterprises:   its annual operational and
investment budgets are reviewed by rho Finance Ministry prior to their execution,
and its net income (after tax and any other pre-approved cash outflows) must be
transferred to the national budget each year. EDPORCHI has a highly developed
and computerized budgeting and accounting systems.  Apart from the one-year
operational and investment budgets that are submitted to the Government every
year, the entity annually updates rolling, long-term (generally 5-year)
investment and financial plans, and capital investments; other important business
decisions are subject to comprehensive economic and financial scrutiny prior to
their inclusion in the entity's plans.
C. lises in the Pgrt Subseclor
1.16      Despite the considerable progr-ei  to date, a few issues remain. These
include:   (a) re±nforcing the coordination between private and public port
investments; (b) strengthening the institutional and regulatory frsmework for the
development of fishing activities in the ports; (c) implmenting measures to
improve environmental port conditions; (d) improving port land access; (e)
restructuring port tariffs a*nd (f) relocation of certain port activities outside
the port areas.
1.17      Reinforcina  the  Coordination  between  Private  and  Public  Port
Investments. The role of the private sector has been very Important in the
development of existing port infrastructure, and Its participation is expected
to be even more significant in the immediate future for the following reasons:
(a) the deliberate policy of the present Government of encouraging maximum
private sector participatio.a in the port sector (para& 1.12); (b) the sizeable
expected increase in port traffic In the iedisate future (para. 1.06); and (c)
the high level of efficiency already achieved In the operation of the Chilean
ports (para. 1.10).   The Government is also concerned about the efficient
allocation of scarea natural resources, such as naturally-protected, deep-water
bays, which are potentially suitable for port development. Another important
matter is the required coordination of operations inside the ENPORCHI ports in
the event a private sector investment is made within thes ports. The Government
has appointed a comission to analyze this and reommend solutions through a
document under proparation entitled lBasid for a Policy of Private Sector
Participacion in the Provision of Port Infrastructure." The main objective of



- 6 -
the document is to establish adequate rules to encourage maximum private sector
participation within a suitable f-amework of competition, economic merits of
irvestment proposals, and adequate coordination with existing transport
infrastructure, environmental conditions, and related urban developments. The
commission has recommended that the Government establish transparent and stable
rules, including setting appropriate port tariffs, to clarify its future role in
the subsector, thus to give confidence to potential private investors.  The
Government plans to review the recommendations and establish a Sector
Coordination Actios Plan, in consultation with the Bank, to define the legal and
regulatory framework to be implemented by the end of 1992. At appraisal, it was
agreed with the Government that, in the event that a future private or public
sector port investment in the hinterland of the project ports is proposed before
the framework is established, the Bank will be informed and will participate in
its analysis, and its comments will be taken into account before the investment
is authorized.
1.18      Streagthenina Institutional Aspects of Fishint Activities In Ports.
Fishing activities in ports, performed by individuals, cooperatives, as well as
industrial companies, have substantially grown in recent years, registering in
San Antonio, for example, a doubling of the fish catch in the 1985-88 period.
This increase is the result of favorable market conditions and better
availability of financing by the Corporation for Promotion of Production (CORFO)
for fishing equipment. This strong development has taken place in a somewhat ad
hoc way, quite often in port areas already congested and with limited capacity
for expansion.   Increasing fishing activities have also contributed to a
deterioration of the environmental conditions of the ports. In addition, fishing
jetties have been generally built by MOP without appropriate guidelines for their
operation, maintenance, and cost recovery. The proposed project provides, as
part of the sectoral component, financing for fishing jetties. A requirement for
this financing will be the completion of a study on fishing development (also to
be financed by the proposed project) that will present recommendations and
actions on the issues mentioned above, and at appraisal it was agreed that a
policy framework and guidelines in this matter, acceptable to the Bank, will be
adopted before June 1993. MOP and the Subsecretariat of Fishing in the Ministry
of Economy (ME) are responsible for taking necessary actions to comply with this
goal.
1.19      Implementinz Measures to Imorove Environmental Conditions in Ports.
As part of project preparation, an Environmental Assessment (EA) of ports in
Region. V and VIII was carried  out by an international,  environmentally
specialized consulting firm, and preliminary results were published in February
1991. The main conclusion of the report is that no adverse environmental effects
are envisaged as a result of the construction and operation of Part A project
components; however, some environmental problems currently exist, as indicated
below, that require immediate attention. For Part B components, an EA should be
carried out for each potential project, and the results will support the decision
on project eligibility. The present environmental conditions of ports in the Vth
and VIlIth Regions varies, with major problems found in: (a) Valparaiso, where
the water quality in the port area is bad because of city sewage discharges,
causing high bacterial contamination in some placeos (b) San Antonio, where the
water is severely polluted by sewage bacteria originated at the San Antonio city
and the Maipo River, and the sediment in the port basin shows very high levels



of copper as a result of inappropriate bulk-handling systems used until recently;
and (c) San Vicente, where the inner part of the bay is heavily polluted with
orgartc matter and bacteria.  The report recommends several actions to improve
the existing environmental conditions, and the proposed project includes an
action-oriented study (Annex 7, B (vi)) to define in detail programs for
monitoring identified environmental problems and for implementing the required
measures. A Plan of Action (Annex 9) has been discussed and agreed in general
terms at appraisal with the Government concerning the environmental problems
related to the project ports. Sewage problems in Valparaiso are covered by the
Valparaiso Water II project (Loan 3331-CH).   The Port Environmental Plan of
Action, to be implemented under the proposed project, is generally addressed to:
(a) implement all necessary actions to ensure the ports are equipped to receive
and dispose of ships' waste; (b) establish the requirements to be complied with
by the fishing sector, to eliminate the waste associated with its activity; (c)
establish an environmental monitoring and evaluation program to support future
decisions; (d) evaluate risks of food contamination by dust (if any) generated
by copper concentrate handled at the port of San Antonio, installing monitoring
devices, and, if positive, implementing actions required; (e) prepare plans to
prevent and control possible environmental damage due to chemical and/or oil
spills in the ports; (f) implement other recommendations included in the above-
mentioned EA; (g) if warranted, dispose of dredging material farther than 12
miles from the coast or at a depth greater than 60 meters; and (h) based on
existing environmental standards to be compiled, prepare environmenta: standards
for ports. The Government plans to implement all these actions before December
1993. At negotiations, this Plan of Action was confirmed and agreed with the
Government.
1.20      Improvement of Port Land Access.   The interaction between a port and
any adjacent city commonly poses very difficult problems to resolve, with their
severity increasing at an accelerated pace over time, and solutions transcending
the  immediate  vicinity  of  the  port  itself.    Social,  urban,  economic,
environmental, land availability, touristic, and aesthetic considerations are
normally factors involved. Valparaiso is a good example, given the commercial
importance of the port, the enormous development of the city, and its unique
topography. A major concern is the land access to this port: at present, trucks
and railways operate in already congested urban corridors, and, for example,
trucks coming from the north drive along the coastal avenue of Vina del Mar, the
most famous beach of the country, and a major tourist attraction. This problem
is further aggravated because the most significant truck cargo from the north is
fruit whose peak period is February and March, which coincides with the tourist
season peak. In the context of a Strategic Study of Greater Valparaiso
(ESTRAVAL), the Commission for Investment Planning in Transport Infrastructure
(CINTRA) is analyzing various alternatives for road and rail access plans for
Valparaiso; also, a similar study is being carried out to improve c;..rtain
transport bottlenecks in the port of San Vicente. The studies of the two ports
are expected to produce preliminary evaluation of solutions by December 1992, and
at that time a Plan of Action on how to implement a Port Access Improvement
Program on those project ports will be presented to the Bank for comments, and
by December 1993, the Government will commence the implementation of the Plan.
The physical components of this program could be financed either under Part B
of this project or under the ongoing Road Sector Project (Loan 3120-CH).



1.21      Restructurina Port tariffs,  Blsed on the present structure of the
public ports system, IMPORCRI levies charges on wharfage, cargo transferred
through its ports, storage, and other services. Tariffs, however, do not reflect
the cost of providing the individual port services, and tariff levels are
generally modified according to the entity's general financial and operational
needs. In an effort to revise EMPORCHI'I tariff system to ensure that tariffs for
the different services rendered are promoting an officient resource allocation
in the port subsector, * port tariff study was carried out in 1988 by the
Catholic University of Chile, financed through loan 2504-CH. The study proposed
a structure bosed on two charges for the use of the port facilities--wharfage and
cargo transferred, which is baed on the cost of port infrastructure and the
degree of congestion at the different ports, as wvll as a separate tariff policy
for the rest of the limited port services provided by EMPORCHI, the latter based
strictly on market considerations, though ensuring coverage of direct and
economic costs.  Due to difficulties encountered in the application of the
consultant's model, the recomamndations were never implemented. The Government
has requested Bank support in completing and implementing the study; hence, loan
funds for this purpose are included in the proposed project. The new tariff
structure will sasure that each of the limited EMPORCHI port activities (para.
1.14) will at least cover short-term marginal costs, and will be utilized to
regulate private ports which are considered to operate under monopolistic
conditions.  During negotiations, agreement was reached that the new tariff
systems will be implemented in EMPORCHI's ports before December 1993.
1.22       R    aligS Outside the Port Area  of Certain Port Activities. At
present, there are som activities performed inside the port that could
conceivably be done outside, with the subsequent advantage of freeing some
valuable areas that could then be used for other port activities, e.g., customs
inspection of containers and agricultural export fruit inspections. Concerning
the former, it ts necessary to distinguish between container shipments with only
one owner of the cargo, or what is called Full Container Load (FCL), which is the
most common case, and container shipments owned by more than one interested
party, known as Le  than Container Load (LCL).  Regulations concerning the
location to perform custom Inspections (inside or outside the port area) are more
flexible for FCL containers. Currently, about 902 of the container traffic in
Chile is handled FCL, and no major varlations in the share are expected in the
future; consequently, customs regulations adversely affect only 10% of the
container traffic. Attempts have been made to liberalize further the system by
permitting custom inspections of LCL outside the port of Valparaiso in a limited
area in the vicinity of the clty--a system that is now under scrutiny. Related
to the overall concept, the proposed project includes a study of inland
intermodal terminals where the custom  issue would be analyzed.  As for the
inspection of fruit exports, well advanced discussions between Chilean exporters
and foreign fruit lmporters are being carried out in the interest of moving this
activity away from the ports. It Is purely a decision between two private groups,
and there are no Government regulations that impede this relocation.
D. Publig Port Soctor Exuendituro Proaram
1.23       Investment, operating, and administrative expenditures in the port
subsector are Incurred by the public as well as the private sector, with an



- 9 -
expected increase in participation by the latter in light of current Government
policy  (para.  1.12).    Obviously,  only  information  on  the  public  sector
expenditure program is available;  and even this is subject to significant
revision in line with the evolution of pLivate investments.
1.24      The MOP's 1992/95 public port expenditure program provides for the
reconstruction of the capacity lost in the ports of Valparaiso and San Antonio
as a result of the 1985 earthquake, the rehabilitation of the Baron berth at
Valparaiso, and the construction of a new general cargo berth at San Vicente.
This program would be partially financed by the proposed project and encompasses
high priority port projects, as analyzed in Chapter II.   The rest of the
expenditure program includes maintenance, replacement, rehabilitation, and minor
works in the ports of the EMPORCHI system, as shown in Table 1.3.
1.25      The overall size of the program,  including the proposed project
components for the period 1992-95 is the equivalent of about US$90.7 million (the
project components represent 572) or about US$22.7 million a year.  This is
deemed appropriate, considering the increasing port maintenance needs as a result
of the aging of port infrastructure, recent earthquake damage, and current and
future demands.  Also, the average annual amount excluding the proposed project
is a1lout US$10.0 million, consistent with the money spent on similar works during
the period 1984-91, which amounted to about US$11 million per year. The total
amount is adequately spread among the ports of the system, and every project is
subject to a detailed economic feasibility analysis and priority ranking before
approving its execution.  Moreover, the size of each project, excluding the
proposed project, is relatively small, on the order of US$1.0 million to US$2.0
million at most (Table 1.3). The expenditure program is in line with port needs
and consistent with the Government policy on port investments. At appraisal,
agreement was reached whereby during the semi-annual consultation meetings, the
Government would review the MOP annual investment program with the Bank;
specifically, before budget finalization, the Government would consult with the
Bank on any proposed new investment project greater than US$5.0 million.
1.26      The proposed project will also include a complementary investment
program for port-related subcomponents that fall outside EMPORCHI's competence
(para. 2.02) but are relevant to port operations. These would include fishery
jetties, intermodal inland terminals, port and inland terminal facilities, and
acquisition of equipment or materials.   The indicative investment program
required will be of the order of US$43.6 million, 67Z of which is expected to be
provided by the private sector. The public components will be executed by MOP
through its technical agencies.
E. Previous Bank Lendina Exnerience in the Transport Sector
1.27      The Bank has assisted Chile in the road sector since 1961.  To date
six loans and a credit, totaling about US$583 million, plus a US$150 million
guarantee for the outer years of a private-sector "B" loan have been granted.
Bank operations focused on: (a) construction, upgrading, and rehabilitation of
roads, periodic maintenance, preparation of feasibility and final engineering
studies, and development of road maintenance capacity through direct technical
assistance as well as provision of funds for procuring maintenance and road
research equipment.



- 10 -
1.28      The objective. of the first two projects--for which Project Completion
Reports (PCRs) and Project Performance Audit Reports (PPAR) were issued in 1979
and 1980, respectively--were not fully realized due to: (a) the effects of major
changes on the economic, political, and social structure of the country; (b)
insufficient funds for recurrent road expenditures; and (c) rampant inflation.
The latest PPAR drew attention to the need for selective organization
strengthenir.g, which was subsequently addressed in the First and Second Highway
Reconstruction Projects.
1.29      With the First Hiahwav Reconstruction Proiect (Loan 1927-CH for US$42
million in April 1981), a very constructive era in Bank relations with Chile
began. The main objective of the project was the reconstruction of about 250 km
of Route Five, mainly south of Santiago (IDB was reconstructing another 500 km).
The January 5, 1987 PCR stressed the project's timely and cost-effective
exocution and commended the National Highway Directorate (DNV) for its management
of the project and its development of modern planning methods. Compliance with
the loan covenants concerning implementation of axle load limit rogulations and
improvement of road maintenance was excellent. All planned weigh stations are
in operation and axle load limit violations have been reduced from about 102 of
all trucks in 1982 to less than 0.52 from 1985 to the present. Several large
private enterprises installed their own weighing stations.
1.30      The Second Hiahwav Reconstruction Pro1ect (Loan 2297-CH for US$128
million in August 1983) was approved to help MOP continue the reconstruction of
435 km of Route Five north of Santiago, as well as the reconstruction and
widening of about 411 km of main transveroal roads. It also helped MOP to otart
a major road maintenance effort of the Basic Network by financing regravelling,
surface dressing, and overlays of about 1,400 km of major roads; construction and
rehabilitation of some 100 bridges (including 60 km of access roads); and
acquisition of road maintenance equipment.
1.31      Lower-than-expected project costs occurred due to the competitive
construction market and the strength of the US dollar in relation to the Chilean
peso. Thia permitted the Bank in 1984 and 1985 to expand loan financing to
accommodate two emergency programs.   The first financed a US$5.6 million
reconstruction program of roads damaged by the 1984 floods. The second financed
repair and reconstruction programs for infrastructure damaged or destroyed by the
March 5, 1985 earthquake. This included a US$12.2 million program for roads and
bridges and a US$15.5 million program for emergency civil works, equipment
procurement, and studies related to repair and reconstruction of the ports of
Valparaiso and San Antonio, as well as rehabilitation of diesel locomotives for
the Railway Company (SFE) and EMPORCHI. Scarcity of counterpart funds during the
years 1984 through 1986 and the execution of the additional works delayed project
completion by more than a year to December 31, 1989. Quality of works has been
excellent, and the revised implementation schedules and original loan covenants
have been complied with fully.
1.32      The First Road Sector Pro1lct Loan 2589-CH for US$140 million in
October 1985, accompanied by a US$300 million private bank "B" loan, of which the
Bank guaranteed the outer US$150 million, partially financed MOP's 1986-89 Roads
Investment and Maintenance Programs, including: (a) construction, reconstruction,
rehabilitation and upgrading of roads and bridges; (b) routine, periodic, and



- 11 -
deferred maintenance of the basic and local road networks; and (c) technical
assistance for coordination of the project, feasibility, engineering and special
studies; works supervision; and training.   The remainder of the program was
financed by IDB, supplier credits, and the Government.
1.33      Under this project, DNV fundamentally strengthened road matntenance
administration and implementation capacity at its headquarters and in the
regions. DNV complied with agreed targets for routine and periodic maintenance
and has established a well functioning information and cost-accounting system
comprising all cost factors, including equipment ownership costs. It also has
carried out a detailed survey of road network conditions using the sophisticated
deflection and roughness measurement equipment procured under Loan 2297-CH. This
effort, coupled with the pavement monitoring and management study, has provided
an excellent base for establishing a Pavement Management System. The training
component has been highly successful, even exceeding agreed targets. The project
was satisfactorily completed, fully disbursed, and closed on June 30, 1990.
1.34      The Second Road Sector Proiect Loan 3120-CH for US$224 million was
approved on September 14, 1989, and is ongoing.   The main objective of the
project is to continue to support the Government's careful financial management
through increasingly efficient execution of its road investment and maintenance
programs. Another objective is the establishment of an effectively functioning
road maintenance and pavement management organization.  It consists of DNV's
1990-93, four-year program of road expenditures, including: (a) investment for
construction, upgrading, and consultant services; (b) periodic and routine
maintenance, minor equipment purchases, and consulting services for supervision,
studies and training; and (c) administration of DNV's program for maintenance and
rehabilitation. The project is progressing satisfactorily, and as of March 1991,
the amount actually disbursed was ju-t 5% lower than forecast at appraisal.
However, some procurement problems have developed recently as a result of the
highly legalistic approach taken by Contraloria General, which delayed the award
of certain contracts.   The satisfactory solution of this problem will be a
condition for Board Presentation.
1.35      In summary, lessons learned during 30 years of Bank lending experience
in the transport sector in Chile are very encouraging. Especially in the last
decade, the country has had an excellent performance fully complying with
objectives, covenants, timing, quality of works and cost estimates of the
different projects. Institutional improvements have been remarkably good, in
many cases exceeding the Bank's expectations. Training programs have also been
highly successful.  Ho-;ever, recent procurement problems that may affect all
sectors (para. 3.14) must be solved to avoid jeopardizing future operations.
1.36      IFC Proiects.  On June 28, 1991, management approved an IFC project
for an investment in the form of an "A" loan of up to US$5 million, a "B" loan
of up to US$12 million, and an equity investment of up to US$5 million for the
expansion of Puerto Ventanas, located about 50 km north of Valparaiso. The total
volume after completion of the project in 1994, is expected to reach 3 million
tons from a current 1.5 million tons. The main traffic at this port includes an
annual import volume of about 1,300,000 tons of coal consumed in the thermal
plant located adjacent to the port, and an export volume of around 500,000 tons
per year of copper concentrate. The project constitutes basically an extension



- 12 -
of the existing pier into the ocean to permit higher ship drafts. Also, another
project for the construction of a general cargo port facility at Coronel, located
about 40 km south of San Vicente, in the amount of up to US$5 million and eauity
investment up to US$3 million, was approved by the Board in mid-1990, but the
project did not become effective and it is considered to be very unlikely it will
proceed, because the private group has lost interest.
II. THE PROJECT
A. Prolect Oriain ond Objectives
2.01      The components of the proposed project at the ports of the Vth Region
were first identified following the 1985 earthquake that seriously d.maged the
San Antonio and Valparaiso ports.  After completion of some emergency works
(financed under loan 2297-CH), which enabled the ports to recover partially
operational capabilities, and before starting any major reconstruction works, the
Government decided to prepare a study on port development plans for the Vth
Region. An agreement was reached with the Government of Japan for the study to
be prepared by the Japanese International Cooperation Agency (JICA), while the
Interamerican Course for the Preparation and Evaluation of Projects (CIAPE)
started the preparation of another study. On completion of the studies in 1987,
the Government, after exhaustively examining them, decided to carry out the
definitive evaluation and preliminary engineering design of the initial phase,
and review the long-term development plans through a final Port Development Plan
for the Vth Region and Feasibility of Phase I. This study was completed in 1988
and is the basis for the Vth Region port investment program to be supported under
the project. Another port development plan for the VIlIth Region, prepared
separately in 1986 and designed to cope with growing exports of forestry products
from the VIIIth to the XIth Regions, is the basis for the other port component
at San Vicente.  Together these projects comprise 57S of EMPORCHI's 1992-95
investment program (para. 1.25). In the supporting feasibility reports mentioned
above, several project alternatives were analyzed, and the selected ones
represent the optimum solutions.
2.02           At the same time, during project preparation the Government and
the Bank recognized that EMPORCHI's public port investment program did not cover
a number of complementary activities that fall outside its authority, nor did it
provide for investments to complement and support private sector port activities
that may develop over the project period but which cannot yet be fully
characterized.   A  "sectoral  loan"  component,  supported by an  indicative
investment program (para. 1.26) and by an economic and environmental criteria
(Annex 3), to select project subcomponents was developed as a result.  At
appraisal, the criteria for this component were agreed.
2.03           The main objectives of the project are: (a) rehabilitating and
modernizing port facilities at San Antonio and Valparaiso, and expanding San
Vicente to cope with forecast traffic, including seasonal peaks, to the end of
the century; (b) increasing efficiency in port activities; (c) alleviating
congestion caused by port traffic in urban areas; (d) promoting wider private-
sector participation in port development and operations, and the introduction of



- 13 -
modern cargo-handling technologies (by private port operators); (e) supporting
institutional improvements in the infrastructure sector; (f) improving
environmental conditions at the project ports; and (g) through a sectoral loan
component, supporting transport sector efficiency and encouraging private sector
development through complementary infrastructure investments. The project fits
well with the Government policy stated in para. 1.12 concerning private sector
involvement in the country.   It supports an even stronger private sector
participation in transport infrastructure through the development of modern
container terminals to be equipped and operated exclusively by private operators,
and through the construction of economically-justified infrastructure, financed
out of the sectoral component, to facilitate the concession of certain port-
related businesses, such as inland intermodal terminals, to the private sector.
The works at San Antonio and Valparaiso ports consist of rehabilitating and
modernizing the facilities loot during the 1985 earthquake, without adding
capacity. Given the special nature of this activity, and as indicated in para.
1.12, it is the Government's policy to undertake this work by itself. During the
last years, major private investments in the San Vicente Port area (expansion of
the Port of Lirquen in 1985, current construction of a new pier for wooden chips
in Puchoco) were not commensurate with the traffic demand. As a result, the
congestion in the Port of San Vicente reached excessive values (902 occupancy
year round, average waiting time of 78 hours/ship) that forced the Government to
undertake on its own the construction of the new berth included in the project.
B. Rationale for Bank Involvement
2.04      The  Bank's  involvement  fits  well  witl  Chile's  outward-looking
macroeconomic framework, and country assistance strategy emphasizes, inter alia,
efficient resource allocation and increased exports, increased private sector
participation in the economy, and improved efficiency in the public sector.
Previous Bank involvement in the port subsector --through sector and project
preparation studies and emergency repairs of the earthquake-damaged ports of San
Antonio and Valparaiso with proceeds of Loan 2297-CH-- has been crucial in
avoiding overdesign and promoting competition among ports as well as changes to
customs regulations and procedurea, mainly with regard to container traffic. In
particular, the Bank's presence in the port subsector would bring a beneficial
catalytic effect in precipitating policy decisions for improvements, which
without Bank participation may be harder to implement given the already high
efficiency level achieved in the systems. This would apply to improvements in
container operations, coordination among relevant Government agencies and with
the private sector (para. 1. 17), orderly development of fishing activities (para.
1.18), and implementation of environmental conditions in ports (para. 1.19).
C. Pro1ect DescriDtion

priority, 1992-95 MOP Port Investment Program, complemented by private sector
investments to make the port components fully operational.  Part B, within a
sectoral context, comprises high-priority, port-related investments already
identified by MOP but requiring further studies to confirm their technical,
economic, operational, and financial justification. In some cases, they will



- 14 -
also need a definition of institutional matters related to them through studies
included in Part A of the project to be financed under the proposed loan.  In
addition, Part B also includes equipment to complement the above, and reflects
private sector investments to develop intermodal transport further. The private
sector has explicitly informed the Government of its willingness and capacity to
provide cargo- and container-handling equipment at the project ports. Maps IBRD
23103 through 23105 show details of the port works. Estimated project costs by
programs are included in the following table, and a su=mary of the project

2.06      The Technical Assistance Program comprises a number of studies,
construction supervision and project monitoring required to fulfill project
objectives and to address the issues raised in Chapter I, Section C in the
following way: increasing efficiency in port activities (paras. 1.18 and 2.03b)
through studies on a Ferry Terminal at Puerto Montt, Institutional Set-up for
Small-Scale Fishing Sector and Fishing Facilities in the Vth Region; alleviating
congestion caused by port traffic in urban areas (paras. 1.20 and 2.03c) through
a study on Port Access Facilities; promoting wider private-sector participation
in port development and operations (paras. 1.17, 1.21 and 2.03d),   through
studies on Development and Implementation of a Port Tariff System, Inland
Multimodal Terminal and Libertador/Los Andes Corridor; supporting institutional
improvements in the infrastructur. sector (paras. 1.22 and 2.03e) through studies
on Institutional Reorganization of the Infrastructure Sector, Macrocenter Region,
and Inland Multimodal Terminal; and improving environmental conditions at the
project ports (paras. 1.19 and 2.03f) through a study on Port Environmental
Standards.  The results of the studies w1il be incorporated during project
implementation as specified, for each project, in the tentative chronograms
included in Annex 7.   When the above-mentioned studies identify specific
investments, provisions are made in the project to finance their engineering,
supervision, and construction. The Technical Assistance Program includes also
the financing of the Project Control Unit to facilitate project implementation.
The project-related training would be addressed to high-level professional staff
of Government agencies concerned with port planning and development and inter.
and multimodal transport. The participation of managerial staff of private ports
and inland terminal operators in training courses, seminars, and site visitti
should be encouraged by MOP and MINTRATEL; however, loon funds allocated to
training would not cover expenses of private participants. In addition to the
above, the training component would include the participation of the
professionals referred to above in international conferences and seminars on port
administration, cargo-handling systems, and intermodal transport, among other.I
Details on the scope, exFected outcomes, and timing of the technical assistance
and training programs agrcsd with the Govern ent are given in Annex 7

D. Pro1ect Cost and Financing
Prolect Cost
2.07      The total project cost, including physical and price contingencies,
as well as taxes and duties, is estimated at US$160.1 million equivalent, of
which US$98.4 million corresponds to foreign exchange costs and US$61.8 million
equivalent to local costs. Bank-financed project components amount to US$131.0
million, representing 82% of the total cost, including contingencies, taxes, and
duties; the difference of US$29.1 million corresponds to the total cost of
project components expected to be financed by the private sector.   Foreign
exchange costs for the Bank-financed project components and for those expected
to be financed by the private sector amount to US$77.7 million and US$20.7
million, respectively. Local costs for Bank-financed and private-sector-financed
project components amount to US$53.4 million and US$8.4 million equivalent,
respectively, in'luding taxes and duties. Taxes and duties amount to US$25.3
million equivalent, of which US$19.2 million will be borne by the Government and
US$6.1 million by the private sector; all will be collected by the former.
2.08      Of the total Bank-financed project of US$131.0 million, US$108.5
million (or 83%) corresponds to the EMPORCHI project components (including
contingencies, tax6s and duties). The foreign exchange costs of the EMPORCHI
components amount to US$62.3 million equivalent and the local costs to US$46.3
million.
2.09      Project base costs are based on: (a) detailed designs for the port
works at San Vicente and the Baron berth at Valparaiso; (b) basic engineering for
San Antonio and the rest of Valparaiso port works; (c) preliminary engineering
for the small-scale fishing ports; (d) preliminary assessment of the rest of
civil works under part B of the project; (e) prevailing prices for container
cranes; (f) prevailing consultants' feea and estimated manpower for professional
services; and (g) leasing rates and estimated operating costs for capital
dredging works (due to the small volume to be dredged, the unit cost is about
three times international prices). Physical contingencies of 10% were considered
for project components, with detailed designs and 5Z was taken for the others.
Price contingencies were calculated assuming annual inflation indices from 1992
to 1998 of 11, 0.5, 0.4, 0.6, 2.7, 3.9 and 4.4%, respectively, after updating
them for the 1991 inflation rate. Forecast local inflation together with local
currency devaluation balances out with foreign inflation; thus, the latter was
also used to calculate local price contingencies. A summary of project costs by
disbursement categories is presented in the following Table, and a detailed cost
estimate is given in Tables 2.1 to 2.3. Also, a detailed cost estimate of the
EMPORCHI project components is given in Table 2.4.

E. Economic Evaluation
2.12      The proposed project would finau.ce key investments of the 1992-95 Four-
Year Public Port Investment Program and other port-related subcomponents included
in a complementary investment program (para. 2.02). It will also contribute toward
introducing reforms and institutional strengthening in areas such as coordination
between private and public port investments, fishint activities 4n ports,
environmental conditions in ports, port land access, and container terminal
management. Consequently, the project's benefits would significantly exceed the
returns on individual investments. Port works amounting to about US$123.3 million
(77S of total project cost) were subject to full economic evaluation prepared by
consultants in 1987 and 1988, and updated by EMPORCHI in May 1991. A provision of



- 20 -
about US$22.2 million (141 of total project cost) has been made for the sectoral
loan components, which will be evaluated during project implementation following the
guidelines established in Annex 3.  Technical assistance and training totalling
about US$14.6 million (9Z of total project cost) will not be evaluated given the
special nature of these expenses. At present, some of the Chilean ports are showing
capacity problems, especially noticeable in Valparaiso during the fruit season
(February, March, and April, when about 801 of the annual volume is exported) and
year round in San Vicente.   This creates port congestion, causing significant
waiting time for ships and trucks. In 1991, average waiting time during the peak
season was about 7 hours/ship in Valparaiso and San Antonio, and 78 hours/ship in
San Vicente year round.   Given the increase expected for foreign trade in the
immediate future (Annex 2) and the already high throughput level achieved mainly by
efficiency and technological improvements (para. 1.10), physical shortages of
capacity will become more severe unless new capacity is added to the system. This
is confirmed by the economic evaluation results.
2.13      For the economic analysis, the port civil works components are divided
into the following individual investments: (i) construction of two container berths,
one in Valparaiso and one in San Antonio, and reconstruction of Baron berth; (ii)
construction of berths l South and 2 as multipurpose in San Antonio; (iii)
construction of berths l and 2 as multipurpose in Valparaiso; and (iv) construction
of a general cargo berth in San Vicente. Benefits were analyzed in each case, and
include savings in either some or all of the following concepts: (a) port handling,
(b) operation and maintenance, (c) ship waiting time, (d) ship service time, (e)
hinterland transport, (f) costs due to port capacity shortage in the without project
situation, and (g) damage costs due to severe earthquakes in the without project
situation. All these benefits were considered in investment (i) above, resulting
in an economic rate of return (ERR) of about 482; for investment (ii), all benefits
except (a) and (d) are achieved, and the ERR becomes 29Z; through investment (iii)
only benefit type (g) is achieved, and the ERR is 181; and the construction of
investment (iv) will only bring benefit of (f) type, with a resulting ERR of about
711. The overall ERR for the evaluated part of the project is 462. Annex 5 gives
details of the economic analysis of each project component and as a whole and
includes a sensitivity analysis. In the most unfavorable combination analyzed in
this analysis (an increase of costs by 151 and a decrease in benefits by the same
percentage), the individu 1 project components continue to be feasible, with an ERR
ranging from 151 to 591    The high returns on these investments are a clear
indication of the urgency in recovering the capacity lost at Valparaiso and San
Antonio because of the 1985 earthquake, the need to prevent future loss of capacity
if another earthquake occurs, and the urgency to provide extra capacity at San
Vicente where congestion is becoming a major problem.
2.14      The major commodities for the project ports are fruit for Valparaiso and
San Antonio and forestry products for San Vicente. As mentioned above, seasonal
variation is extremely important for fruit traffic, while it is less significant for
forestry expor'i. The concentration of fruit exports is so high that the capacity
requirements of the first two ports is determined by the volume moved in March. As
part of the evaluation of likely economic alternatives for Valparaiso and San
Antonio, the possibility of reducing the seasonal effect of fruit exports was
analyzed, details of which appear in Annex 5. The main conclusion is that world
market conditions for grapes and apples, the main fruit exports, require very strict
deadlines for the Chilean fruit to be competitive; consequently, the shift of the



- 21 -
fruit shipment outside the peak period seems highly unlikely. With respect to using
alternative means of shipping fruit, i.e., airfreight, containers or lighterage, the
analysis concludes that they should be discarded because the cost involved would be
substantially higher.
F. Financial Asoects
2.15      Since EMPORCHI wovld be the beneficiary of 68S of the project (79Z of the
loan), a complete analysis of its past and projected financial performance was
carried out and is presented in Annex 6. The analysis was carried out both for
EMPORCHI as a whole and for each of the project ports, separately. The ultimate
purpose of the analysis is to ensure that the entity, in its capacity as an
autonomous public enterprise, would be in a position to finance out of its own
resources, after covering operating *xpenses, its share of the project and the
servicing of the corresponding portion of the Bank loan. The analysis also covers
a description of the institutional and regulatory setting of the entity, cost-
recovery aspects, including the adequacy of its tariff system and the mechanisms to
be established for the flow of the counterpart funds, through the Government, to
MOP's budget in its capacity as executing agency. The most relevant considerations
are summarized in the following paragraphs.
2.16       Under the existing legislation, BWORCHI operates as a self- sufficient,
commercial, state entity responsible for the exploitation of eleven commarcial
ports, except for the provision of stevedoring services, using sound financial
principles. It operates in an environment of market competition with the private
sector in the provision of both port infrastructure and services. It obtains its
revenues from charges for wharfage, cargo transferred through its ports (although
not handled by the entity), storage and other services. Its main expenses are
salaries for employees involved in storage and administrative activities and
maintenance of facilities. EMPORCHI, however, cannot dispose of its net profits,
SO of which are paid to the Government in the form of income taxes and the other
50% transferred to the national budget.  An exception is made for (authorized)
investments in port infrastructure, where the cost of works and/or goods to be
purchased can be deducted from the mounts to be entered into the national budget.
2.17      The analysis of the entity's financial results over the last nine years
(1982-90) shows EMPORCHI as a highly profitable institution with an average working
ratio (defined as total working expenses divided by total operating revenues) of 362
and an average operating ratio (working expenses plus depreciation divided by total
operating revenues) of 54Z. Its averago annual revenues have amounted to about
US$48 million and its average annual net income before taxes to about US$16 million,
or 33% of revenues. Transfers to the Government in the form of income taxes have
averaged US$8 million p.a. (consistent with the 50 income tax rate), but transfers
to the national budget have avoraged US$11.0 million p.a., thus exceeding EMPORCHI's
net income after taxes. Consequently, to comply with the national budget's
requirements for funds, EMPORCHI has transferred to the Government not only its net
income after taxes but also part of its depreciation reserves.
2.18      EMPORCHI's financial results are expected to remain sound over the next
decade (1991-2000). Its average working ratio would rmain at no higher than 46%
level during the forecast period, and the average operating ratio would be about 51%
over the same period. This performance would be the rosult of a projected 3. 5% p. a.



- 22 -
average increase in traffic, and therefore in revenues, which would offset minor
cost increases expected under some expense headings.
2.19      EMPORCHI's cash position should also remain healthy over the forecast
period. The entity would generate annual operating cash surpluses (revenues less
operating expenses), increasing gradually from the equivalent of US$29.0 million in
1991 to US$36.0 million by the year 2000. This internal cash generation together
with the depreciation reserves and the proceeds from the Bank loan would place
EMPORCHI, after meeting its tax obligations, in a very favorable position with
regard to project financing.   Indeed, in every year during the forecast period
(1991-2000), the entity will be in a position to fully finance its part of the
investment program, including both the Bank project and its own investment
requirements. Moreover, except for 1992 and 1993, it will be able to continue to
transfer 100Q of its net income (after tax) to the national budget. During 1992 and
1993, however, when the bulk of the project investments are expected to be executed,
EMPORCHI will need to suspend the transfer of funds to the national budget on
account of profits. At the individual ports' level, during 1992, 1993, 1995 and
1996, San Antonio's cash generation will not fully cover the counterpart funds
required for the works at this port, and, therefore, internal transfers from those
ports generating surpluses will be needed. However, with the pick-up in traffic
and, therefore, in revenues, which is expected at San Antonio as works are being
completed, the increase in this port's cash generation, starting in 1997, will more
than compensate for the deficits of those years.
2.20      The forecast shows that no increase in tariff levels in real terms would
be required to meet the entity's financial needs. At appraisal it has been agreed,
however, that a restructuring of the present tariff structure, to ensure that
tariffs are contributing to an efficient resource allocation in the port subsector
under an environment of market competition with the private sector (para. 1.21),
will be implemented before December 1993. Assurances were obtained at negotiations,
that the new tariff system, satisfactory to the Bank, would be adopted by December
1993.
2.21      In addition to the above, even though the entity's past and projected
financial situation undoubtedly reflects excellent operational and financial
management, at negotiations, the Government agreed to include the following
financial covenants to ensure continuity of present practices during the forecast
period (1991-2000): (a) EMPORCHI will achieve working ratios no higher than 462; and
(b) EMPORCHI will produce funds from internal sources for each fiscal year
equivalent to, at least, cover its share of the project cost (48% of the total cost
of the EMPORCHI project components), after meeting cash operating expenses, debt
service (including for the proposed loan), increases in working capital, and other
significant cash outflows other than capital expenditures.



- 23 -
G. Project Risks
2.22            All port project components would be designed applying the most
advanced seismic design criteria to ensure that all structures would withstand the
most severe earthquakes that may occur in the region. Since 1978, projects in the
highway subsector have been timely and efficiently executed with no cost overruns
by MOP'o DNV. However, this is the first Bank operation in the port subsector in
Chile; therefore, there is no experience with the capacity of the DOP of the MOP,
the executing agency for all civil works, to implement the project properly and on
time. To minimize this risk, the project would provide for the establishment of a
Project Control Unit (PCU) (para. 3.04) to assist the existing IPC during the
implementation of the project.
2.23      The risk of environmental hazards during construction would be prevented
by clear contract specifications concerning disposal of dredged materials, anr
strict contractors' compliance with traffic restrictions imposed by the municipa;
authorities at the project ports on transit of heavy machinery and construction
equipment and materials through urban areas.
III. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION DETAILS
A. Status of Preparation
(i) Part A Components
3.01       Most of Part A components are well defined, as feasibility studies and
basic engineering for all port works (77% of total cost) were completed in 1988 and
updated in 1991.   Construction works at San Vicente and the Baron berth at
Valparaiso are already underway; preparation of bid documents for port works at San
Antonio is well advanced; and engineering for all other Valparaiso port works has
started.  The environmental monitoring and controls (1% of total cost) will be
determined as a result of the study on port environmental standards financed by the
project; the container cranes (13% of total cost) would be provided by the private
sector, but the main characteristics were defined by consultants retained to assist
EMPORCHI in the definition of the operational features of the container terminals.
Draft terms of reference for all studies included in the project were prepared by
agencies responsible, and they were discussed and agreed during appraisal. Brief
summaries are included in Annex 7.
(ii) Part B Components
3.02      The port-related investments under Part B --such as construction of
wharves for small scale fishing ports at various locations along the coast,
improvement and construction of port and inland access facilities, construction of
inland terminals and complementary investments to facilitate private sector
investments-- would require additional studies or decisions to determine if they are
eligible for financing under the project.   The eligibility of investments as
consistent with the agreed criteria (Annex 3) would be discussed and reviewed during
project irlwlementation at the semiannual consultation meetings to ensure that they
are  technw-cally  and  environmentally  sound and  economically  justified.    The
probability is high that sufficient investments of this kind will go forward, and



- 24 -
the flexibility thus introduced mote than offset the cost of the related commitment
fees.
B. Reggonwibilities of Ahencie, Involved
3.03      DOP of MOP will be the executing agency for all port components, which
represents more than S0  of the project.  Some other minor components will be
carried out with the cooperation andlor policy or technical guidance of other
agencies  (MOP, MINTRATEL, ME, SECTRA,  etc.).   EMPORCHI,  as owner and final
beneficiary of the additional and (re)conetructed and rehabilitated berths, will
participate in the decistont made for such works through its involvement in the I?C
and IPCU (para. 3.04). IMPORCHI will also be responsible, through the Ministry of
Finance and following mechanism. satisfactory to the Bank, for providing counterpart
funds during the construction period, and make available to the Government of Chile
the cost of the Project as financed by the Loan. The specifics of the financial
responsibilities of EMPORCUI to cover fully the cost of the Loan to the Government
of Chile were discussed and agreed in general during appraisal. At negotiations,
the Government and EMPORCII presented a satisfactory draft of the agreement to be
signed between them specifying the Government's (through the DOP) responsibilities
in the execution of the key Program investments, as well as EMPORCHI's rights and
responsibilities during project implementation in repaying such investments and the
timing thereof; also, it covers the conditions for the transfer of ownership of such
investment to EMPORCHI. The signing of the atreement and its effectiveness will be
a condition of loan effectiveneas.
3.04      As indicated above (pars. 1,12), there are several agencies involved in
the port sector and their coordination at the general and policy level is effected
by IIC, with the port coaaission, IPC, responsible for handling more specific port
matters. The system is *ffoctive and permits a broad discussion of problems for the
purpose of reaching consensus in important decisions; however, the successful
implementation of the project would require a coordination body specifically
appointed for it.   Consequently, the Government has decided to establish an
Interministerial   Project   Coordination   Unit   (IPCU)   including   high-level
representatives of all agencies concerned with the project (IIC, IPC, EMPORCHI, DOP,
MINTRATEL, MIDEPLAN, Ministry of Finance), with the following functions: (a) act as
counterpart of the Bank and participate in the project's semi-annual consultation
meetings; (b) propose and request definitions on relevant matters to the
corresponding agencies; (c) perform general coordination of the project,
specifically of the work assigned to the different agencies; (d) supervise and
monitor the general progress of the project; (e) take all necessary actions to
implement the recommendations of the technical assiestance studies; and (f) overview
the preparation of periodic reports to the Bank.  To complement this set-up, a
Project Control Unit (PCU) was created to coordinate and monitor the execution of
the project on a daily basis; this unit is headed by a full-time professional with
qualifications satisfactory to the Sank, supported by two full-time professionals,
one part-time advisor and required support staff. The PCU will act as a liaison
with the Bank through the IPCU. Chart 4 shows the functional relationships among
IPCU, PCU, agencies involved and the Bank. The functions, responsibilities and
staffing of the PCU were discussed and agreed at appraisal, and during negotiations,
it was agreed that its complete staffing is a condition of effectiveness. The PCU
has already been established, and its chief has been appointed.
