C. Prolect Execution
3.05      The proposed project would be completed in about seven years.  Chart 5
indicates the timing of the different activities involved in the project.   At
appraisal, an understanding was reached on the expected dates for hiring consultants
to carry out the studies, their completion, and the implementation of the
recommendations emanating from the studl.es. During negotiations, agreements were
reached on implementing the recommendations of the following studies in this manner:
(a) Institutional Set-up for the Fishing Sector (para. 1.18) by June 1993; (b) Port
Environ!mental Plan of Action (para. 1.19) by December 1993; (c) Port Access
Improvement Program (para. 1.20) by December 1993; and (d) Port Tariff System (para.
1.21) by December 1993. The schedule for the preparation of detailed engineering
for Part B works that comply with the eligibility criteria, to be financed under the
loan, would be reviewed at the semi-annual meetings.
3.06      The training component addressed to intermodal transport and transport
infrastructure and planning would mainly be under the responsibility of IIC, DOP
EMPORCHI and MINTRATEL, the main beneficiaries of this component. It is expected
that the private sector will be encouraged to participate in some of the training
activities, particularly with regard to intermodalism. At appraisal the following
was discussed: (a) the annual training programs outlining specific objectives,
beneficiaries, activities, and their location; (b) the selection criteria for
scholarship candidates and working visits; and (c) the format of annual reports on
training to be prepared during project execution. All these activities would be
reviewed at the semi-annual consultation meetings.
3.07      At  negotiations  it was  agreed  that within  six months  of project
completion, the Government, with the assistance of all agencies involved, would
prepare a Project Completion Report (PCR) to assess the success of the project,
including the achievement of institutional, administrative, operational, and
financial objectives and goals, and general evaluation of the lessons learned from
project experience.
D. Proiect Implementation Aspects
(i) Private Sector Involvement at Proiect Ports
3.08      The Government has decided that all container-handling equipment required
for the efficient operation of the container terminal 3 at San Antonio and Valparaiso
should be provided by private operators in accordance with the following framework,
discussed during appraisal and confirmed at negotiations: (a) the terminals would
be operated following the system of multiple operators currently used at Valparaiso;
for that purpose, EMPORCHI will open a special registry of operators that, having
applied for licenses to provide their services at the terminals, comply with all
financial, technical and operational requirements established by EMPORCHI.  All
licensed operators would be obligated to provide their service to whoever requires
them; and (b) the provision of container cranes by private operators would be done
through international competitive bidding procedures that would establish the
qualification requirements to participate (financial, technical and operational),
and the bid evaluation criteria will be based, among others, on the following
aspects: commItment by the bidder to provide ship-loading!t'nloading services to all



- 26 
port users applying published tariffs previously agreed with EMPORCHI, that would
be reviewed from time to time; capacity, age, productivity and maintenance plans of
the proposed cranes.   In addition to the above, bidders would have to submit a
proposal to install and operate additional cranes, when and if traffic justifies it.
(ii) Location of the Containe)' Yard at San Antonio
3.09      The development of the container terminal at San Antonio requires the
construction of a container yard;   EMPORCHI and MOP carried out an economic
evaluation of the two possible alternatives: namely, at the site currently occupied
by fishing and chemical industries (which would, in any case, have to be relocated
when berth 4 is built around the year 2000), and at an area to be reclaimed behind
the existing breakwater at the back of the container berth 3. During appraisal, the
mission reviewed and found satisfactory the economic evaluation, which concluded
that the least-cost solution is to reclaim the necessary area behind the existing
breakwater to build the container yard; accordingly, the Government confirmed to the
mission its decision to include in the project the construction of the container
yard at that site.
(ii) Relocation Aspects
3.10      The construction of berth 3 in San Antonio will require the relocation
of the fishing fleet operations currently being carried out at that site.  At
appraisal, it was agreed that this activity will temporarily operate on floating
pontoons at the south end of the port basin, and later will be moved to the fishing
facility to be financed under the project. During negotiations it was agreed that
the Government would terminate the authorization for the fishing fleet to operate
at berth 3 by March 31, 1992, under the assumption that construction works would
start on May 31, 1992. If this date slips, the date for the ceasing of the fishing
activities would be adjusted accordingly.
E. Procurement and Consultancv Services
3.11      Procurement of goods and works under the proposed project would be carried
out in a manner consistent with the Bank Guidelines (May 1985). Contracts for civil
works that would be financed retroactively (para. 3.16) were awarded using ICB and
LCB procurement procedures and bidding documents satisfactory to the Bank, prepared
by MOP in accordance with Bank Sample Bidding Documents. Draft bidding documents
for the procurement of goods, also based on the Bank samples, are currently under
preparation, and would be reviewed by the Bank as soon as they are available; it
would be expected that the final version, satisfactory to the Bank, will be
available well before 1993, when procurement of the first goods included in the
project would start. Consulting services would be contracted in accordance with
Bank Guidelines for Use of Consultants (August 1981).  Contracts for consultant
services for the detailed design of the main port works at San Antonio and for the
provision of technical assistance to EMPORCHI in the definition of the operational
features and layout of the container terminals at San Antonio and Valparaiso were
awarded following procedures established in the Bank Guidelines, using terms of
reference and conditions of contract satisfactory to the Bank and are being financed
under 3120-CH.



- 27 -
3.12      For Parts A and B of the project, contracts for civil works estimated to
cost US$3.0 million or more, and equipment purchases of US$250,000 or more would be
procured through ICB.   Contracts for civil works estimated to cost between
US$300,000 and US$3.0 million, and equipment purchases estimated to cost between
US$50,000 and US$250,000, would be awarded on the basis of LCB procedures acceptable
to the Bank, subject to an aggregate limit of US$22.0 million for works, and US$1.5
million for equipment and materials. Contracts for less than the above limits would
be procured on the basis of a minimum of three quotations from qualified suppliers,
subject to an aggregate limit of US$4.0 million for works and US$0.5 million for
equipment and materials.   The thresholds established for ICB would ensure that
contracts awarded through ICB could account for about 58% of the total estimated
project cost and about 72Z of the Bank-financed project components. About 18% of
the project cost corresponds to project components expected to be financed by the
private sector, using procedures other than the Bank's; however, given the incentive
to minimize cost, it is expected that procedures used by them would be on the basis
of sound procurement practices. The Table in the next page shows a breakdown of
project costs, including items expected to be financed by the private sector, and
loan amount by procurement method.
3.13      The Bank's prior review of procurement documentation for civil works and
goods will cover all major procurement steps (advertising, bidding documents,
evaluation reports, award recommendations, and contracts), and would apply to:
(a) the first two contracts for goods and for works under ICB, and all
contracts exceeding US$4.0 million for works; and
(b) the first two contracts for goods and for works procured under LCB.
The Bank will also review all documentation (terms of reference,
evaluation of proposals, award recommendation and contract) for all consulting
services included in the project. The limits established for prior Bank review of
procurement decisions will result in a coverage of at least 60% of the estimated
project cost, including private sector investments, or 75% of the cost of the Bank-
financed project costs. In the case of equipment to be purchased under Part B of
the project by the Borrower and his agencies, the precise equipment list and most
appropriate bid packaging and related procurement arrangements, consistent with Bank
procurement guidelines, would be identified by specialized consultants during
project implementation. A margin of preference of 15% of the CIF bid price of goods
or thie amount of the customs duties, whichever is lower, would be granted for
purposes of bid evaluation for procurement of goods under ICB.

3.14      Procurement problems which have affected ongoing Bank-financed projects,
are: (a) differences between the Government and the Bank as to what constitutes a
substantial deviation with respect to the bidding documents to justify the rejection
of a bid; and (b) restrictions imposed on professionals who have not validated their
University degrees in Chile. With respect to the first problem, the Government,
through the DNV, has simplified and revised the bidding documents in a manner,
satisfactory to the Bank, to reduce to a minimum the risk of rejecting a proposal
for deviations from the bidding documents, whose correction would not substantially
change the scope, contents, quality and price of the bid concerned. Concerning the
second point, on October 23, 1991, the Chilean Congress approved a law eliminating
the requirement of title validation for professionals working in a project financed
by an international organization like the Bank when required by the loan agreement.
Assurances were obtained confirming the understanding that this law is applicable
to the loan. Consequently, the two procurement problems are satisfactorily solved.



- 29 -
F. Disbursements
3.15      Disbursements of the Bank loan weuild be made against:
(a) 49S of total expenditures of civil works;
(b) 100S of the foreign exchange expenditures of imported goods; 1002 of the
ax-factory cost on locally-manufactured goods; and 772 of local
expenditures for imported goods locally procured;
(c) 1002 of total expenditures, excluding taxes, for professional services;
and
(d) 10OZ of total expenditure., excluding taxes, for training.
The closing date for project disbursements will be June 30, 1999.
3.16            Funds from the Bank loan are expected to be disbursed over a period
of seven years closely in line with the standard profile for transport projects in
the Region. Retroactivo financing in an aggregate amount up to US$7.1 million
(equivalent to 1OZ of the proposed loan), with respect to payments made for
expenditures incurred within the 12-month period before loan signature, but not
before March 4, 1991 (date the Bank initiated the appraisal process), would be made
with respect to port works at San Vicente and the Baron Berth at Valparaiso (para.
3.01), and for consultants' services for technical assistance components including
supervision of construction. At appraisal, all disbursement-related matters and
retroactive financing have been discussed and agreed, in principle, with the
Government and were confirmed at negotiations. An estimated schedule of
disbursements is given in Table 3.1.
G. Special Account and Auditing
3.17      To maintain an adequate flow of funds to finance eligible project
expenditures with a minimum of administrative delay, the Government would establish
a Special Account in the Central Bank under terms and conditions satisfactory to the
Bank.  The Bank would, upon the Government's request, deposit an aggregate amount
equivalent to a maximum of US$6.0 million (4 months of estimated expenditures) from
the loan account into the Special Account. Records of the Special Account proceeds
and outlays would be available for review by Bank supervision missions and subject
to annual audit. Disbursements would be made on the basis of certified Statement
of Expenditures for civil works where the contractual values are less than the
equivalent of US$500,000 and for goods where contractual values are less than the
equivalent of US$250,000.   The corresponding supporting documentation would be
verified on a routine basis during the supervision process.
3.18      EMPORCHI's financial statements are annually audited by the Contraloria
General de la Republic&. Given that the scope of the auditing work required by the
Bank goes beyond that performed by the Contraloria, it has been agreed with the
Government that during project Implementation, private independent auditors,
acceptable to the Bank, will be appointed to perform the external audit for EMPORCHI
and DOP's project accounts. At appraisal, agreement was reached on the external
auditing of EMPORCHI by private auditors in accordance with appropriate auditing



- 30 
principles and on the forwarding of the annual audit reports to the Bank not later
than six months after the end of each fiscal year.  The audit will include the
Special Account.
H. Environmental Impact
3.19            The  Environmental  Assessment  (EA)  of  the port  component  that
constitutes the core of the project, carried out by the National Agency of
Environmental Protection (NAEP) under the guidance of the Ministry of the
Environment of Denmark, concluded that there would be no adverse environmental
impact as a result of the works to be executed at the project ports nor by the
transit and handling of the commodities at the facilities to be financed under the
loan. However, the EA identified a number of existing problems, as well as some
port activities that need to be monitored to detect, measure, and prevent or reduce
adverse effects to the local environment (Annex 9).  In addition, construction
contract specifications would stipulate in a manner satisfactory to the Bank,
dumping sites for dredged material at the project ports, and restrictions to prevent
other environmental hazards during construction. Each Part B component will require
a specific EA to determine project eligibility (Annex 3). The project would include
technical assistance to compile port environmental norms and standards, and
monitoring instruments and equipment to tackle the problems whose solution is under
the responsibility of the Directorate of Maritime Territory and Merchant Marine
(DIRECTEMAR) and EMPORCHI, in accordance with their respective functions.
I. Proiect Monitoring and Semiannual Consultations
3.20            Monitoring of the project by the Bank would be as follows:  (a)
annually, starting in mid-1992, review the progress of the Action Plans, the
implementation of the recommendations emanating from the technical assistance
components, the progress and results obtained from the training program and the
achievement of financial covenants; and (b) hold semiannual consultation meetings
with the IIC and the IPC (around March and September) to review the progress in the
implementation of the port component, and review the eligibility of sectoral
components in light of the specific studies included in the project, to define the
budget and programs for the next year.  During negotiations, these matters were
discussed and agreed with the Government and its relevant agencies.
IV. AGREEMENTS TO BE REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
4.01            At aD2raisal, agreements were reached with the Government on the
following, which was confirmed at negotiations:
(a) inform the Bank and take its comments into account before authorizing any
port investment in the hinterland of the project ports (para. 1.17);
(b) scope of a study to develop and implement the regulation of fishing
activities in the port areas to reduce pollution and congestion, and
establish the framework for an orderly growth of these activities before
June 1993 (para. 1.18);



- 31 -
establish the framework for an orderly growth of these activities before
June 1993 (para. 1.18);
(c) plan of action for implementing environmental improvements in ports
before December 1993 (para. 1.19);
(d) preliminary evaluations of solutions for project port accesses will be
ready by December 1992, and at that time, an improvement program for port
land accesses will be discussed and agreed with the Bank (para. 1.20) and
begun to be implemented by December 1993;
(e) implementation of a new tariff system in EMPORCHI will be done by
December 1993 (para. 1.21 and 2.20);
(f) scope of a study to pursue expansion of customs inspection and clearance
to inland terminals (para. 1.22) to be submitted to the Bank by December
1992;
(g) discuss and review during the semiannual meetings MOP's annual port
investment programs before they are authorized, specifically with regard
to new investment projects greater than US$5.0 million (para. 1.25);
(h) methodology to determine economic, environmental, and financial viability
for sectoral components (para. 2.02 and Annex 3);
(i) scope and timing of the professional services to be included in the
project and details of training (paras. 2.06, 3.05 and 3.06);
(j) project costs (para. 2.07);
(k) working ratios, and caeh generation requirements to be achieved by
EMPORCHI (para. 2.21);
(1) functions, responsibilities and staffing of the PCU satisfactory to the
Bank (para. 3.04);
(m)  current authorization to the fishing fleet to operate at berth 3 at San
Antonio will be terminated on March 31, 1992 (para. 3.10);
(n) procure goods and services in accordance with Bank guidelines (ICB) and
LCB procedure", satisfactory to the Bank (para. 3.11);
(o) terms and conditions, and auditing procedures for the project, including
the Special Account (para. 3.18).
4.02      Full staffing of PCU (para. 3.04), and the signing of the agreement
between  Government  and  EMPORCHI  (para.  4.02),  will  be  conditions  of  loan
effectiveness.
4.03      Subject to the above, the project provides a suitable basis for a Bank
loan of US$71.0 million. The terms would be 17 years with a 5-year grace period.

- 46 -
ANNEX 1
Page 1 of 5
CHILE
TRANSPORT INFRASTRUCTURE PROJECT
The Transport Sector
A. General
1.        The backbone of the entire Chilean transport system (Map IBRD 23102)
is Route Five, a section of the Longitudinal Road that runs from the Peruvian
border in the north to Quellon on the island of Chiloe in the south. Between
Iquique and Puerto Montt, Route five is paralleled by rail. The transport system
also consists of a rather dense string of ports, which can be used for national
and international shipping, as well as a series of airports for local and
international traffic. Road transport represents about two-thirds of domestic
freight, railways carry one-fifth; coastal shipping and oil pipelines combined
carry about one-tenth; and air transport handles less than 1%. Some 95% of
international trade in tons is seaborne (some 90% of exports and about 80% of
imports by value). Passenger traffic is mainly by bus, although there is some
rail service.
B.  The Roads Subsector
2.        The Network.  The road network covers about 79,000 km, of which some
11,000 km (about 141) are paved either with cement concrete (3,600 km) or with
asphalt. Because of the country's long and narrow shape, the network is dominated
by three major stretches of north-south road (almost 5,000 km in total): the
Lonigitudinal Road (Road 5), from the First to the Tenth Region; the Austral Road
(Road 7), between Puerto Varas (north of Puerto Montt in the Tenth Region) and
Villa O'Higgins in the Eleventh Region; and Road 9 from Paso Baguales, on the
Argentine border of Torres del Paine, to Fuerte Bulnes, south of Punta Arenas.
Some 6,000 km of longitudinal and mayor transversal roads comprise Chile's
National Roads, which, with the Regional Roads, constitute the Basic Network of
some 23,000 km. The country also has some 56,000 km of Local Roads.
3.        Traffic. Annual average daily traffic measured at major toll stations
on the National Roads fluctuates currently between 2,000 and 9,000 vehicles
except in the extremities of the country. It grew yearly between 2.4% and 18.3Z
in the 1978-88 period, except for declines by 3.4%, 11Z and 3.8% during the
depression of 1982 and 1983, and in 1985, respectively. In the last four years,
1987 through 1990, the annual traffic increase has been above 10%. The share of
passenger cars in traffic increased until it reached 68% in 1983, whereas the
share of trucks decreased. Since 1982 the share of trucks, particularly with
three or more axles, has increased considerably.
4.        Road Motor Vehicle Fleet. The registered, motorized road-vehicle
fleet, after a very rapid increase in the late sixties and in the seventies, is
now growing more slowly. It reached about one million in 1988, or about 77
vehicles per 1,000 inhabitants, versus about half that amount in 1976.



- 47 -
ANNEX 1
Page 2 of 5
5.        Road User Charges.  The current structure of the road user charges in
Chile is generally adequate. Total revenues from taxes on road transport are
(over US$600 million equivalent per year in the 19800) between two and three
times road expenditures (some US$225 million equivalent per year), but there is
some justifiable cross-subsidization between private cars and trucks. Further
improvements to the system to -eflect price variations as well as to be used as
an instrument to d6.rease congestion and discourage axle overloads are being
introduced under Loan 3120-CH.
6.        Weight  Control  Regulations.  In  the  early  1980s  the  Government
introduced a sophisticated system of weighing-in-motion, that considerably
reduced truck overloading in the country to an average of less than 0.5Z at
fixed stations. Further efforts to improve enforcement of vehicle axle load
limits are currently being pursued by the Government in certain forestry areas
where violations still persist.
7.        Road Maintenance and Pavement Manaaement. Under the last three Bank
(road) projects, Chile's Ministry of Public Works successfully established a
modern      road  maintenance   and  pavement  management   organization  and
institutionalized the use of the Highway Design and Maintenance Standards Model
(HDM). This effort has placed Chile among the leaders (world-wide) in the
preparation of highway investment and maintenance programs for both the country's
internal use as well as for the preparation and execution of Bank road sector
projects. The application of the model is currently being expanded to all the
regions in the country.
C. The Ports Subsector
8.        The Port System   Chile has more than 5,000 km of coastline, along
which there are about 70 port installations, excluding fishing jetties and
passenger terminals, of which some 26 are commercial ports involved in
international traffic. Of the latter, 11 are publicly owned by EMPORCHI and 15
are private. The EMPORCHI ports are all general cargo ports. Except for Lirquen
port in the Concepcion area, which also handles third-party traffic, the rest of
the major non-EMPORCHI ports are used by industries and serve specific state and
private interests.
9.        Port traffic.  The 26 comercial ports account for some 90S of all
port traffic. The remaining 10 percent is primarily comprised of bulk liquids
from the southern-most Twelfth Region where the Chilean oil fields are located.
In 1990, the commercial ports handled some 28.6 million tone of international
traffic, 39Z of which was handled by ENPORCHI ports (Table 1. 1). The trend of the
international traffic in the 1980. has been a rather flat behavior of around 17
million until 1985, followed by a vigorous increase of about 14.52 pa. onwards
(Chart 1). National coastal traffic reached about 9.2 million tons in 1989,
showing a similar trend to the international traffic during the 19809.



- 48 -
ANNEX 1
Page 3 of 5
10.       In 1989 and 1990, EMPORCHI ports handled some 11-12 million tons of
international traffic and around 2 million tons of national coastal traffic per
year (table 1.2), which represents respectively around 39Z and 22X of the total
volume moved by the country. Almost 80S of that tonnage passes through four
ports (Antofagasta, Valparaiso, San Antonio, and Talcahuano/San Vicente ports).
In exports, two-third of EMPORCHI vzlume is handled, in roughly equal parts, by
San Vicente (forestry products) and by Valparaiso/San Antonio (most general
cargo, fruit and copper); three-quarters of EMPORCHI imports (general cargo) are
moved through Valparaiso/San Antonio; about 80% of the international transit
traffic passes through Arica and Antofagasta; and about 40Z of EMPORCHI's
national coastal traffic is handled at Antofagasta and San Antonio.
11.       In value terms, EMPORCHI's share of the total Chilean port traffic is
substantially higher than its percentage based on tons, because the company
handles at least 90S of all general cargo in the country. Even though the non-
EMPORCHI ports are free to handle any traffic (their own or that of third
parties), except in the Concepcion area, where the private port of Lirquen is
growing rapidly, the handling of third party traffic is limii:ed. Moreover, most
of the private port facilities are specialized terminals handling low to medium
value bulk cargo.
12.       During the 1980s, container traffic has increased significantly by
almost fivefold, reaching in 1989 a value of over 2.1 million tons in about
177,000 containers (124,000 loaded and 53,000 empties), all of which were moved
through the EMPORCHI ports. Of the total amount, Valparaiso accounted for about
104,000 (inciuding 25,000 or 24Z empties) or almost 60Z. Container traffic is
expected to experience a significant growth in the next decade (see Annex 2).
13.       Retulatory Framework. Chilean ports are controlled by the Navy, under
the jurisdiction of DIRECTEMAR for ship movements, and MINTRATEL (mainly through
EMPORCHI), for commercial operations. Port construction is the responsibility
of the DOP of MOP and EMPORCHI.
14.       Port Tariffs.  Based on the present structure of the public ports
system, EMPORCHI levies charges for wharfage, cargo transferred through its
ports, storage and other services. Tariffs, however, do not reflect the cost of
providing the individual port services. In an effort to revise EMPORCHI's tariff
system to ensure that tariffs for the different services rendered are promoting
an efficient resource allocation in the port subsector, a port tariff study was
carried out in 1988 by the Catholic University of Chile and financed through loan
2504-CH. The study proposed a structure based on two charges for the use of the
port facilities --wharfage and cargo transferred--which would take into
consideration basically the cost of port infrastructure and the degree of
congestion at the different ports, and a separate tariff policy for the rest of
the port services, the latter based strictly on market considerations and
ensuring coverage of d'rect and economic costs. The actual restructuring of
EMPORCHI's tariffs or second stage of the study is being included in the proposed
project.



- 49 -
ANNEX I
Page 4 of 5
15.       Port Staffinz. Following the institutional restructuring of the early
1980s, EMPORCHI reduced its total number of staff from 3,193 in December 1980,
to 1,394 (56Z) by end-1982. The most important reduction was that of port
operators as all stevedoring functions were transferred to the private sector.
The ratio of operators to professional and clerical staff was 8:1 in 1960, fell
to 2.4:1 by 1975 and to a mere 0.35:1 in 1988. Of course, the change in skill
mix reflects the new orientation of EMPORCHI as a regulatory rather than
executing organization. The total number of employees remained at about 1,400
until 1986/87 when staff was increased to slightly over 1,800 to meet the ports'
increasing need for security personnel.
16.       Port Operations.    All EMPORCHI ports are operated by private port
stevedoring companies under concessionary agreements, except for wharfage and
storage, which remains EMPORCHI's responsibility. All cargo handling equipment,
as well ad one of the gantry cranes in Valparaiso, are provided by private
operators. The rest of the gantry cranes are owned by EMPORCHI, but have been
transferred to private operators under lease agreements. EMPORCHI is basically
responsible for providing and maintaining port infrastructure and for
coordinating the activities of the different port users in the port area.
D. The Railway Subsector
17.       The Network. Chile has about 6,903 km of track providing service to
the general public of which 3,989 km are broad gauge (1,676 mm), mainly the
railway south and west of Santiago, and the rest is meter gauge (1,000 mm),
mainly in the north.   About 1,800 km of this track is electrified: namely,
towards the south to Temuco, to the west to Valparaiso and part of the way to San
Antonio. About 745 km of the track is welded, mainly to the south. The railway
is badly deteriorated, as negligible amounts were spent on the repair and
maintenance of the infrastructure, motive power, and rolling stock in the past
two decades.
18. Traffic and ODerations. Railway traffic has been declining. Currently,
freight traffic is around 6.5 million tons per year or about 1.8 billion ton-km.
The number of passengers moved is about 8 million.   Traffic in the Southern
network is heavily concentrated in a few bulk commodities. Mining and forestry
products account for about 80X of the traffic in tons, and nine products (logs,
copper, cellulose, coal, steel, wheat, cement, fertilizers, and sugar) produce
852 of the total tonnage. The volume of forestry products for exports (including
logs) is increasing rapidly and has promising potential for exploitation by
railway.
19. Recent Reforms. Vigorous reforms of the railway system were initiated in
the 1970s. In continuation of these reforms, the 1980s saw the Railway Company
(EFE) undergo further trimming, and a drastic reduction in size took place, more
in keeping with its economic role; staff levels were drastically cut, from about
27,000 to less than 7,000 by mid-1986; operating losses were reduced by 502 in
real terms, and Government subsidies were reduced gradually from an average of
US$50 million per year in the 19709 to almost zero in the 1980s. However, in
spite of the quite remarkable achievements, EFE's losses continued, and in 1986



- 50 -
ANNEX I
Page 5 of 5
EFE's debt peaked at some US$125 million mainly because of its reluctance to
reduce the size of its passenger services and its large assets that continued to
be incompatible with its long term economic role. A vigorous program to sell
unremunerative assets in the last few years reduced the debt to the present US$88
million.
20.       More recently, passenger services have been substantially curtailed,
and a decision has been made at the highest levels of Government to seek private
sector participation in the management and operation of the railways. Although
the exact form in which privatization will be done is not yet decided, serious
efforts are underway to restructure the railways.
E. Airports
21.       Airport Infrastructure. Chile has an adequate number of airports,
about 128 in total, of which twenty-one have commercial activities, and seven are
used for international flights. No major expansion plans are envisaged at this
time in the subsector, except for an expansion of the international passenger and
cargo terminals in Santiago for which bids have already been called. The
construction of a proposed second runway for Santiago has been postponed
indefinitely in view of its dubious economic justification at this point.



- 51 -
Page I of 3
CHILE
TRANSPORT INPRASTRUCTURE PROJECT
Current *nd future Traffic in Proiect Portg
1.        As indicated in Annex 1, the total port traffic in Chile has shown a
substantial increase in the 1980., especially in the period 1985-89 when the
foreign trade in tons increased at an annual rate of about 14.51, and the
national traffic at more than 8S per year. In the same period, the number of
tons moved in containers more than doubled.   The current Government has
underscored its intent to maintain the ongoing overall economic policy framework,
endorsing the concept of an open, free-market-oriented economy vith special
emphasis on exports. The overall result. and indexes obtained so far suggest the
immediate prospects for the economy are good, and consequently a continuation is
expected of the significant increase in transport demand registered during the
1980s.
2.          Estimates of the traffic forecast for the ports of the project
during the period of economic analysis are discussed below. In all cases, the
forecast is based on specific estimates made for the major comodities moved by
each port, and responds to very conservative assumptions made on the expected
evolution of the traffic demand.
A. Proiect Ports of the Vth Renion
3.        The total traffic moved through the two main ports of the Vth Region,
Valparaiso and San Antonio, increased from about 3.7 million tons in 1987 to
approximately 5 million tons in 1990, equivalent to an annual traffic growth rate
of over 102. The share of the different traffic categories moved through these
ports have remained relatively stable during the last years, reaching the
following percentages: 351 of imported general cargo, 202 of fruits, 201 of
exported general cargo, 121 of copper, 8S of cabotage, and a combined 51 of
wheat, corn and transit traffic. The percentages of containerized cargo has also
shown a significant increase in the last few years, reaching in 1990 the
following values: 471 of imported general cargo tonnage, 701 of exported general
cargo tonnage, and 37S of cabotage.
4.        In 1986, detailed traffic projections were prepared for both ports by
a consultant as part of the studys "Plan de Dcearrollo Portuario Vs. Region y
Factibilidad la. Etapa," and the estimtes have been updated by ENPORCBI in March
1991. The first task was to compare the previous projections for the period
1987-90 with the actual values. The total traffic was higher than originally
projected in the base case, showing increases of 61 in 1988, 51 in 1989 and 91
in 1990. In the last year, the major icreases were registered in general cargo
(312 for imported, almost double for exported), which was partially offset by
decreases in fruit (91) and copper (30). In the caso of frult, the originally
projected values for 1987 and 1988 represented the real values almost exactly,



- 52 -
ANNEX 2
Page 2 of 3
but the significant difference occurred in 1989 with a decrease of about 100,000
tons, or approximately 12Z, with respect to the original forecast. This was
mainly due to the problems caused by the charges presented in the US against
Chilean grapes (in Chile known as the "Case of the Poisoned Grapes"), which was
never proven and consequently is not expected to have any significant effect on
future exports. For instance, in the following year (1990), the exported volume
of fruit already showed a significant increase of 11.52 over 1989.   Copper
exports were lower than forecast almost exclusively in refined copper, which were
the result of some problems in the production of two mines, El Teniente and
Enami, which are in the process of being solved.
5.        The methodology followed by EMPORCHI to update the traffic forecast
was basically to use the real traffic values reached in 1990, and then apply the
original methodology used by the consultant, modified as needed to reflect recent
developments and available information. The general results are presented in
Table 1. Details can be found in the report, 'Proyecciones de Carga Puertos de
la V Region, Valparaiso-San Antonio," EMPORCHI, March 1991, which is included in
the Project File.)
6.        Traffic assignment to Valparaiso and San Antonio was also done in the
same report taking into consideration the results of computer models used by the
consultant, differential values of land transport cost, degree of attractiveness
of each port, and historical trends. The results are included in Table 2, and
details can be found in the above mentioned report.
B. Port of San Vicente
7.        The traffic in the Port of San Vicente in the VIIIth Region has
increased from about 1.7 million tons in 1985 to over 2.8 million tons in 1989,
which represents an annual growth rate of over 132. The traffic mix has
consistently shown an increase of the share of forestry products, which in 1989
reached over 70X of the total San Vicente traffic,  the rest being mainly
fertilizers, fishmeal, and saltpeter. San Vicente is the most important port in
the Concepcion area, moving about 422 of the port traffic in this area, and has
reached 902 utilization in 1990 with an average per ship waiting time of almost
4 days.  The ratio of average ship waiting time over its berth service time
resulted in around 0.75, or about three times the recommended UNCTAD value for
normal port operating conditions. Congestion conditions in San Vicente are
becoming a real problem.
8.        In 1987, detailed traffic projections for San Vicente were carried out
by consultants as part of the study "Ampliacion de la Capacidad Portuaria de la
VIlIth Region."   Those projections were updated by EMPORCHI personnel in June
1990, given the dynamic behavior of the port demand in the last years, and the
significant participation of some products, notably chips, that were only
starting to be exported at the time the report was prepared. The forecast was
carried out in detail for forestry products that are as noted the main commodity
for San Vicente, while the remaining products were forecast mainly on an
historical basis.



- 53 -
ANNEX 2
Page 3 of 3
9.        The traffic  forecast  is based on the installed  and anticipated
production capacity, local consumption, required inputs, and availability of
forestry product resources in the San Vicente port hinterland of the following
items: cellulose, paper, panels, sawn wood, wood for pulp and chips, and logs.
The first three products use wood for pulp and chips, whereas sawn wood uses
logs. Finally, the difference between the volume produced and locally consumed
for each of the six items yields the exportable volume; what will finally be
forecast as exports depend on this amount, international prices, and the expected
conditions  of the world market.    Table  3 depicts  the production,  local
consumption, export and exportable excedent forecast of forestry products, while
Table 4 gives the traffic forecast for San Vicente, including non-forestry
products.

CHILE
TRANSPORT INFRASTRUCTURE PROJECT
Economic and Environmental Eligibilitv Criteria for
Sectoral Components (Part B)
I. Economic Criteria
A. Screening Process
1.        The identification of potential projects for inclusion in the proposed
Loan is based on these principal sourcest (i) the continuous infrastructure needs
search conducted, on a regular basis, by the Executive Secretariat of the IIC;
(ii) the research and evaluation work being carried out by agencies such as:
CINTRA, SECTRA, DNV, DOP, EMPORCHI, DIRECTEMAR, MIDEPLAN, Subsecretaria de Pesca,
etc.; and (iii) the Regional and local authorities which also observe
deficiencies and/or identify infrastructure requirements. Upon receipt of the
different ideAs, the IIC will screen the identified projects, select those which
are most likely to be included in the program and apply the following economic
evaluation procedures to confirm project feasibility, optimize project design and
determine budgetary priorities.
B. Types of Projects Subject to Economic Evaluation
2.        In general terms, an economic evaluation is to be conducted at varying
levels of detail depending upon project scope and degree of certainty of project
feasibility, for the following types of projects:
* Port improvements;
* Conetruction of fishing berths;
* Improvement of port access roads;
* Supporting infrastructure at selected inland terminals;
* Other infrastructure works;
* Goods.
C. General Methodology for Economic Evaluation
3.        General principles are included in the publication: "Inversion Publica
Eficiente, un Continuo Desafio", March 1991 by NIDEPLAN, which should be taken
into consideration for any evaluation. Some more specific aspects are included
below, which should also be taken into consideration to evaluate economically
potential projects. Some general observations are made (especially in para. 4)
that could be applicable to other infrastructure works.
4.        Port  Improvements.  In  general,  the benefits  generated  by  port
improvements can be identified as savings (comparison of the with and without
project situations) in either some or all of the following categories: (i) port
handling costs; (1i) operating and maintenance costs; (iii) ship waiting time
and other costs due to port capacity sLortage; (iv) ship service time costs; (v)



- 59 -
ANNEX 3
Page 2 of 4
hinterland transport costs; and (vi) seismic cost. The without-project situation
should reflect no significant new investments except the ones either under
construction or soon to start, but will include improvement on existing
conditions in such a way to set a "strong" without project situation.   Cost
during construction should be included as part of the analysis. Generally, the
economic evaluation is based on normal traffic, but special cases may justify the
inclusion of generated traffic, if significant. If relevant, traffic projections
will include:   normal, pessimistic and optimistic cases.   In the economic
analysis, the appropriate number of alternatives should be considered, and each
meaningful subsystem from the operational viewpoint should be evaluated on an
incremental basis.   If relevant, stage construction will be analyzed as an
alternative. During this marginal analysisS each increment of investment that
proves to be feasible should integrate the without-project situation for the
following analyses. For major projects, it may be advisable to utilize computer
models to simulate port activities. When relevant, special consideration should
be given to seasonal variation of demand and to determine appropriate transport
or non-transport means of reducing its effects in project design.
5.        Construction  of  Fishing  Berths.    The  study  on  administrative,
operational and financial features for small-scale fishing ports will recommend
the appropriate methodology to: (i) assess the expected attitude of future users
to utilize this type of infrastructure, and (ii) evaluate this kind of project,
from the economic and social viewpoint. If subsequently approved by Government
and Bank, it will be used to assess the merits of these projects.
6.         Improvement of Port Access Facilities.  The main benefits of this
kind of project go well beyond the pure port-related benefits, because they may
include urbanistic, touristic, city traffic and safety, environmental, social,
and historical benefits, which quite possibly may largely exceed the mere port
benefits. Nevertheless, these may be significant in terms of savings in vehicle
operating costs and time of the trucks (and eventually rail trains) accessing the
port under current severe congestion conditions. Congestion is not covered by the
HDM III model (Highway Design and Maintenance Standards Model, developed by the
World Bank), but this matter is at present being studied by DNV. One possible way
of reducing the impact of the city on port truck operating costs is to study the
possibility of prohibiting the operation of the trucks during the city peak
hours, which undoubtedly might create another kind of disadvantage to the port
truckers. Normally, the investment for this kind of project is very expensive
because of the high degree of urbanization in the project area, and its
feasibility can only be analyzed in the overall urban context. Consequently, the
project includes a comprehensive urban study on access roads to the project
ports, which will include the economic evaluation of alternatives in a broad
urban context.
7.        Supportina Infrastructure at Selected Inland Terminals. The proposed
loan may finance the provision of transport, power, and water supply at selected
freight inland terminals which, according with Government decisions, would be
built by the private sector, after proving their economic feasibility. The
provision of those facilities would eventually be considered as an incremental
investment to the main project, and as such their benefits would be the expected



- 60 -
ANNEX 3
Page 3 of 4
savings by comparirig the project alternative(s) with the without-project
situation, i.e., using the existing transport, power, and water facilities. In
some circumstances, the latter may be impractical, in which case the facilities
should be considered an integral part of the main project; in this situation, the
feasibility of the whole project, including the cost of providing transport,
power and water, should be the only requirement.
8.        Goods. The economic analysis will be based on comparing benefits and
costs as differences between the with- and without- project situations.
D. Major Analytical Parameters
9.        Feasibility Indices. Sensitivity and Risk Analysis. For each project,
an estimate of the First Year Benefit/Cost Ratio, Net Present Value and the
Internal Rate of Return should be prepared. To test the sensitivity of the
evaluation, critical variables are to be adjusted in accordance with possible
alternative scenarios. In case land user time savings were included as benefits,
the indices should be calculated with and without that value.
10.       Shi, Time Saving Values. Liner vessel tariffs are normally set by the
liner conference system, and consequently are somewhat inelastic (at least in the
short to medium run) to any reduction in ship waiting and service time in
specific ports.  Therefore, the benefits to Chile of those kind of improvements
may not be fully realized soon.  As a result, the daily ship cost of liner
vessas. will be taken, conservatively, as 50% of the corresponding values
periodically published by the World Bank applying the Shipcost Model. For charter
ships, tariffs are negotiated on a case-by-case basis; consequently, the effect
of port improvements are perceived almost immediately. Therefore, for charters
full-ship costs per day should be taken.
11.       Economic and Shadow Prices. For each project two basic evaluations
will be carried out: economic and social. The economic evaluation considers all
costs and benefits measured in market prices reduced by the corresponding taxes
and duties. The social evaluation also considers other factors relevant to the
economy, which translates into a set of adjustment factors periodically prepared
by HIDEPLAN. The opportunity cost of capital will be 121 in both cases, unless
revised by the Government and the Bank.
12.       Period of Analysis. The period of analysis will be consistent with the
expected life of the proposed infrastructure project.
13.       Optimum Times For each project the optimum time (maximum value of NPV
at the opportunity cost of capital) should be established.
II.  Environmental Criteria
14.       For addressing the environmental effects of Part B components of the
project, the "Guidelines for Environmental Assessment (EA) of High-Priority
Infrastructure Projects", MIDEPLAN (Attachment 1) are adequate, insofar as they



- 61 
ANNE 3
Page 4 of 4
condition all future subprojects to a process oft (a) environmental screening
to assess the potential environmental hazards, and (b) provide general terms of
reference for the required EA studies of such projects.  These requirements
mirror closely the Bank's own requirements for environmental screening and
assessment!' of sector loan projects in which the specific subprojects are not
identifiable at the time of loan approval. Hence, all works to be carried out
under Part B components of the project will be subject to environmental screening
according to the MIDEPLAN TORs, and financing of these components will be
conditioned on Bank and MIDEPLAN clearance of the EAs for these subproject
components.   All subprojects will be required to satisfy both the Bank and
MIDEPLAN requirements for environmental screening and assessment.
III. Proiect Selection and Execution
15.       Based  on the  results  of economic  feasibility  reports  prepared
following the guidelines mentioned above and on satisfactory EA of each project,
the selection of potential projects to be financed by the proposed loan would be
discussed and agreed at the regular semiannual consultation meetings as part of
project implementation. As a selection criteria, projects would be considered
candidates if they show an ERR greater than 122 and if possible environmental
impacts are minimized to acceptable standards. Works that do not qualify under
the established criteria could be replaced by new ones as agreed between the
Government and the Bank.
16.       Once the project is approved, agreements will be reached between the
Government and the Bank on procurement procedures, executing agencies and the
legal bases for this.
1/   The Bank Guidelines for EA for all sectors are contained in the
following Bank docuwents: (a) Annex "A" of the Operational Directive
(OD) 4.00, and (b) Environmental Assessment Sourcebook Volumes I & II.

CHILE
TRANSPORT INFRASTRUCTURE PROJECT
Guidelines for Environmental Assessment (EA) of
High-Priority Infrastructure Prolects l
1.        Among the requirements to finance high-priority project components yet
to be fully identified is the necessity to carry out an EA for each of them.
Since location and scope of each component may have different environmental
impacts, it would be necessary to develop an EA methodology appropriate to the
specific characteristics of each one to ensure that:
(a) all elements that may effect the environment are identified and their
impact in improving or deteriorating it are evaluated; and
(b) actions and/or measures necessary to prevent, reduce and/or eliminate
existing and potential environmental conditions are proposed.
2.        Based on the above,  all infrastructure projects proposed  to be
included in the Government investment plans, will require a preliminary EA to
identify all possible causes of positive or negative environmental impact on
human beings and animal species, either through water, air, or soil pollution,
and verify their potential impact against internationally accepted standards.
3.        After completion of the preliminary EA, a detailed EA should be
carried out, if required, including a review of impact on the bio-geophysical,
ecological, socioeconomic and cultural aspects as listed below:
(a) Bio-geophysical and ecological:
- air, water, soil, noise and ecosystem
(b) Socioeconomic and cultural:
- present and future land and natural resource utilization and conditions;
- human settlements' relocation;
- lifestyle changes induced/caused (i.e., increased vehicle traffic, urban
congeetion, etc.)
- temporary increase of project-generated employment and its influence on
the job market;
- project-induced variations of real estate value;
- archeologic, historic, artistic and tourism sites; and
- overall infrastructure and services.
1/   The. F- -nish version entitled: "Linsamientos para la Evaluacion de loo
Atractos Ambientales de lo Proyectoo Sectoriales del Propuesto Proyecto
Sectorial de Infraestructura I (Transporte)", MIDEPLAN, is included in
the Project File (Amnex 10).

4.        To be used as  plannlng tools, the EAs should include alternative
actions for the achievement of the environmental objectives at the least possible
cost. In addition, the EAs should be used as an important element in project
evaluation and selection.

CHILE
TRANSPORT INFRASTRUCTURE PROJECT
Detailed Proiect Description
1.          The proposed project, which would be carried out during a seven-year
period bEtween 1992 and 1998, includes two parts:
Part A: comprises MOP's 1992-95 high-priority Port Investment Program,
complemented by private sector investments to ensure that the port works included
in the above program become fully operational upon their completion. In addition,
this part of the project includes technical assistance and training to: (i)
determine the economic, financial and technical feasibility, as well as the
institutional features of other high-priority port-related investments included
in Part B of the project; and (ii) review and redefine institutional objectives
and functions of the Government's infrastructure sector agencies; and
Part B: comprises other high-priority MOP's port-related investments,
complemented by private sector investments to develop further intermodal
transport.
The following is a detailed description of the components included in the
project:
Part A - 86.22 of the project cost
1. San Vicente (Map IBRD 23105) (5.92 of the project cost)
- construction of a general cargo berth (berth Cero), including filling,
paving and utilities of the back up area;
- supervision of construction of the berth Cero;
- acquisition of equipment and instruments to improve (by the private
sector) and monitor the environmental port conditions;
2. San Antonio (Map IBRD 23104) (42.32 of the project cost)
- conatruction of a container berth (berth 3) to replace the remains of
the existing one damaged during the 1985 earthquake, including paving and
utilities of the back up area;
- construction of a multipurpose berth to replace berths 1 South and 2
that collapsed during the 1985 earthquake;
- reclamation of about 4.6 Ha. at the seaward side of existing breakwater,
including the construction of a protection dike, for the development of
the container yard and inner roads described below;
- construction of a container yard, including container terminal
building.;



- 65 -
ANNEX 4
Page 2 of 3
- construction of port inner roads to serve the port area to be developed
under the project;
- dredging to deepen the port basin and the areas alongside berths 1, 2
and 3, and the port basin to a depth of -12m;
- construction of a fishing wharf to relocate the fishing fleet currently
using the remains of berth 3, which will be replaced by the new container
berth;
- supervision of construction of above civil works;
- acquisition of equipment and instruments to improve (by the private
sector) and monitor the environmental conditions, including monitoring
devices to measure level of copper concentrates that may be generated by
the existing bulk terminal located at berth 1;
- acquisition and erection of container cranes by private port operators
(6.3% of the project cost).
3. Valparaiso (Map IBRD 23103) (32.5Z of the project cost)
- reconstruction of berth 3 to convert it into a modern container berth,
including demolition of existing old sheds and buildings, and existing
pavement to construct a container yard;
- construction of a container yard, including paving, utilities and
container terminal buildings;
-reconstruction a berth 2 to convert it into a modern multipurpose berth,
including paving and utilities of the back up area;
- reconstruction of existing general cargo berth 1 to realign it with the
new berths 2 and 3, including paving and utilities;
- rehabilitation of the existing Baron berth to enable it to handle mainly
fruit exports during the peak season;
- adapting existing quay cranes for use on reconstructed berths;
- dredging to deepen the areas alongside the new berth 1, 2 and 3, and
the Baron berth;
- supervision of construction of above civil works;
- acquisition of equipment and instruments to improve (by the private
sector) and monitor the environmental port conditions;
- acquisition and erection of container cranes by private port operators
(6.62 of project cost);
- detailed engineering for berths 1 South, 2 and 3;
4. Technical Assistance (5.02 of the project cost)
This project component consists of consultants' services to:
(i) determine the economic, financial and technical feasibility of high-
priority MOP's port-related investments included in Part B of the project;
(ii) define the institutional aspects of the small-scale fishing ports,
the Libertador-Los Andes Corridor and inland freight terminals;
(iii) review and redefine the objectives and functions of the Government's
infrastructure sector agencies;



- 66 -
ANNEX 4
Page 3 of 3
(iv) study and make recommendations on port tariffs, access roads and
environmental standards;
(v) carry out the engineering design for fully justified high-priority
investments included in Part B of the Project;
(vi) support the PCU.
5. Training (0.5Z of the project cost)
(i) scholarships for, and working visits of professional staff of the
Government agencies concerned with port planning and development, and
inter- and multimodal transport;
(ii) courses and seminars in the country and abroad for the professional
staff referred to in proceeding paragraph and private port and inland
terminals' operators/investors;
(iii) travel expenses and fees for attendance of professional staff of
Government agencies to international conferences on port administration,
port planning, dedicated terminals and cargo-handling systems, and
intermodal transport.
Outline terms of reference and scope of the consultants services and
details of the training component are given in Annex 7.
Part B - 13.8Z of the project cost
- construction and/or upgrading port access roads in the urban areas
affected by port traffic at the project port.l
- construction/upgrading access roads to sites where the private sector
decides to develop inland freight terminals;
- construction/modernization of small-scale fishing ports, including
wharves, operating and working areas and ancillary building;
- construction works to provide water supply and power to sites where the
private sector intends to develop inland freight terminals;
- construction of the Libertador-Loo Andes inland terminal;
- construction of inland freight terminals by the private sector (4.82 of
the project cost); and
- acquisition of equirment and materials required to complement high-
priority port-related works previously described.

CHILE
TRANSPORT INFRASTRUCTURE PROJECT
Economic Evaluation of Port ComDonents
A. Background
1.        In 1986 and 1987, feasibility reports of the major project component.
were carried out by consultants, and in early 1991, they were updated by EMPORCHI
incorporating relevant changes in the main parameters of the analyses; these
documents form part of the Project File (Annex 10). The following is based on the
results of those studies modified as necessary to reflect the appraisal mission's
judgement.
B. General Methodolcoav
2.        Economic analyses were carried out for a number of selected sub-
projects on a marginal basis and for the project as a whole. For this purpose,
it was necessary to define individual components in investment increments which
represent a meaningful sub-system form the operational viewpoint. The analyzed
sub-projects are the followings
(i)  construction of two container berths one in San Antonio and one in
Valparaiso, as one single sub-project given the complementary roles
of the two ports for this kind of traffic, and reconstruction of Baron
pier in Valparaiso to operate as a reserve capacity when Valparaiso
is under construction;
(ii) construction of multipurpose general cargo berths at San Antonio;
(iii) construction of multipurpose general cargo berths at Valparaiso;
and
(iv) construction of a new general cargo berth at San Vicente.
The first three sub-projects were analyzed in sequence, and the definition of the
without project situation in each case included the previously analyzed sub-
component if found to be feasible.
C. Main Characteristics
3.        The aim of the analysis is to define a short term program to satisfy
the demand by the turn of the century for the ports of both Regions. In this
short period, the without project situation is characterized as follows: (i) no
new bertha are constructed, (ii) existing berth are not reconstructed, (iii) no
major repairs intended to extend the life of existing structures are undertaken,



- 68 -
ANNEX 5
Page 2 of 4
and (iv) maintenance and repairs necessary to preserve existing facilities are
routinely performed. The damages produced by the 1985 earthquake in the ports of
the V Region have shortened the life of some existing facilities to few year.,
which was contemplated in the analysis. Thus the aggregate number of berths in
the ports of Valparaiso and San Antonio was estimated to be 16 in 1991 and 1992,
14.5 in 1993 and 1994, 13 in 1995 and 1996, dropping to a minimum of 11.5 from
1997 onwards.  The decreasing trend is worrisome especially considering the
expected significant traffic growth in these ports.   In the event of the
consideration of a new project in the influence zone of the projects, it should
be analyzed on an incremental basis considering that, for this analysis, the
without project situation will include all the components found feasible and
financed by the project.
4.        Traffic assignment for the ports of Valparaiso and San Antonio
presents an interesting case because the two ports handle basically the same kind
of cargo, they are located close to each other (about 60 km apart) and the land
transport networks feeding to each port are very similar. In the feasibility
report of 1987, it was proved that the transport costs of connecting each traffic
generator with either port were practically the same and therefore are not
determinant of traffic assignment to these ports.   In the latest study bw
EMPORCHI, the criteria for assignment of the different kinds of vessels (full
container, bulk carriers, reefer and multipurpose) to the different types of
berths in each port was to minimize the aggregate congestion cost.  Special
congestion curves (waiting time vs. 2 of utilization) were derived for different
number of berths available and for each kind of vessel, and they were calibrated
with statistical data of the port of Valparaiso. The final result is close to
a 50-50 percent split between the ports, but obviously would depend, to a certain
extent, on decisions to be taken by the shipping companies.
5.        For the container berths sub-project, the following benefits for the
peak and non peak months, were considered:
(i)  hinterland transport cost savings in moving the boxes from/to the
ports to/from final destination/origin;
(ii) port handling cost savings which comprises  savings in the cost
(lifting, moving through the terminals and overhead costs) of handling
containers in the new specially-designed container berth as compared
with the costs of handling them in the existing not specially-design
container berths;
(iii) ship service time cost savings through the utilization of high speed
cranes which will reduce ship berthing;
(iv) ship waiting time cost savings as a result of greater service capacity
and higher speed in the with project situation, which will undoubtedly
reduce the probability of ships waiting for berth space;



- 69 -
ANNEX 5
Paga 3 of 4
(v) capacity shortage cost savings because the project will provide
increments of capacity which will help to alleviate or eliminate those
expected shortages and thus reduce the extra costs (in terms of
diversion   to   other   ports   or   other   costly   decisions   by
exporters/importers) which would otherwise be associated with the
inability to move the cargo through the ports; and
(vi) seismic risk benefits because in the event of a major earthquake, the
aseismic nature of the project structures will assure more remaining
capacity in the with project situation, thus avoiding or reducing the
extra costs associated vith a complete collapse of the ports.
6.        Liner vessel tariffs are normally set by the liner conference system,
and consequently are somewhat inelastic (at least in the short to medium run),
to any reduction in ship waiting and service time in specific ports. Therefore,
the benefits to Chile of those kind of improvements, may not be fully realized
at the beginning. Thus, to calculate the benefits in the economic analysis, the
daily ship cost of liner vessels was taken, conservatively, as 50% of the
corresponding values published from the World Bank Ship Cost Model. For charter
ehips, tariffs are negotiated on a case-by-case basis; consequently, the effect
of port improvements are perceived almost immediately. Therefore, for charters
full-ship costs per day was taken.
7.        The general cargo berth at San Antonio will add capacity to the system
because as a result of the 1985 earthquake the existing facility where the new
berth will be built is not operational. Consequently, the type of benefits, for
the peak and non peak months, will be hinterland transport cost savings, ship
waiting time cost savings, capacity shortage cost savings and seismic risk. This
kind of project will not generate port handling and ship service time benefits
as the container berths sub-project did, because those concepts will not change
between the with and without project situation.
8.        The general cargo berth at Valparaiso will not add capacity to the
system because the existing berth is currently operational. The basic benefit
will be the reduction or avoidance of the extra costs associated with a major
earthquake which in the without project situation could very likely produce a
complete collapse of the port, while with the project the aseismic nature of the
structure could almost completely avoid the disaster. The project will also
generate some minor benefits in hinterland transport cost and negative benefits
due to congestion during construction. This sub-project is supposed to be started
in 1995, and a salvage value was added at the end of the period of analysis to
represent the present value of the remaining life of the structure beyond the end
of that period.
9.        The general cargo berth at San Vicente will add badly needed new
capacity at the Concepcion area, and the main benefit will be reduction in
congestion conditions in the area which is calculated in terms of savings in ship
waiting time.



- 70 -
ANNEX 5
Page 4 of 4
10.       All costs and benefits used in the analysis are based upon expected March
1991 prices and shadow-priced as necessary. The latter are based on periodic review
and publication by MIDEPLAN. The opportunity cost of capital was assumed to be 122
and the period of analysis is 1991 to 2010.
II.       A major conclusion of the 1987 feasibility report for the Vth Region is
that, for the general cargo berths, most of the berth requirements result from the
demand for fruit shipping capacity during the peak month, March, when the fruit
traffic volume represents about 352 of the annual value.   Consequently,  the
consultant carefully analyzed whether that peak might be modified. Two basic
possibilities were considered: (i) shift the shipping date of some of the fruit away
from the peak period, and (ii) provide alternative means to move the fruit during
the peak and thus reducing the demand on the required number of berths. For
different reasons, both possibilities appear to be unfeasible. With respect to the
first one, two types of fruit account for the March peak: apples and grapes, and the
peak results from the fact that March is the end of the grape season and the
beginning of the apple season. In the case of grapes, the U.S.A. is the major
customer, and it requires that April 20 is the deadline to receive grapes, and as
such, the product must be loaded aboard ship and depart Chile no later than April
5. Although the date is subject to change based on political pressure, the view of
Chilean exporters is that it is unlikely to happen. With respect to apples, a major
selling point for Chile is that its crops matures earlier than that of its major
competitors, i.e. New Zealand and South Africa. For this reason, the best price is
received for the earliest shipments, and there is constant pressure to ship at the
earliest possible date. In summary, the shift of the fruit shipment outside the peak
period seems highly unlikely. With respect to the second possibility mentioned
above, alternative means of shipping fruit could be the use of airfreight,
containers, or lighterage, but analyses conclude that they should be discarded
simply on commercial grounds.
D. Results of the Economic Evaluation
12.       In the analysis it was assumed that containers will mainly be transported
from/to Chile in full container ships, but about 800,000 tons will continue to be
moved by multipurpose ships, as is happening now.
13.       Tables I through 4 present the results for the different sub-projects
analyzed. The economic rates of return (ERR) were high for the individual units of
analysis as well as for the project as a whole, ranging from about 182 for the
general cargo berth at Valparaiso, to 712 for the general cargo berth at San
Vicento. The construction of two container berths at Valparaiso and San Antonio,
which represents about 602 of the total port civil works, has an ERR of 472. The
overall ERR for the port components of the project is 46S.
14.       The high returns obtained for the different components reduce the need
for carrying out elaborate sensitivity analyses to test the solutions. Nevertheless,
a sensitivity case was run increasing by 152 the investment costs, reducing by the
some percentage the benefits, and a combination of both cases, and the ERRs remain
acceptable (Table 5), ranging from 152 to 592 i: the most demanding combination.

CHILE
TRANSPORT INFRASTRUCTURE PROJECT
Einancial Analysis of EMPORCHI
A. Introduction
1.        This annex presents an evaluation of EMPORCHI's past and projected
financial situation, including its capacity to finance its share of the project
and to service the corresponding portion of the Bank loan.The discussion covers
the following main aspects: institutional set-up, costs and tariffs systems, past
financial performance, future financial performance, project financing plan and
financial conditionality for the project.
B. Institutional Set-up
2.        EMPORCHI was created in 1960 (Law No. 290 of the same year) to replace
the "Servicio de Explotacion de Puertos" which belonged to Customs in the Finance
Ministry. It was placed under the Transport Subsecretariat of the Ministry of
Economy, which in 1977, developed into the MINTRATEL. By law the task of EMPORCHI
was the exploitation, administration, and maintenance of the ports under its
jurisdiction. In addition, EMPORCHI had exclusive rights over receiving, handling
ard storing goods within the port areas and for handling over these goods to the
owners. It was also authorized to rent and/or grant concessions for the use of
port property for periods of up to ten years.
3.        By the end of thL 1970's, Chile was   facing a substantial labor
monopoly in the ports and efficiency had deteriorated to a point where ship
queues were reaching alarming proportions. To tackle the above situation, by the
end of 1981, the then Government promulgated two laws (Nos. 18,032 and 18,042):
the f.:st, opening the profession of port worker to anybody who could obtain a
pex,nt from DIRECTEMAR, and allowing the workers to offer their services either
independently or to institute join private stevedoring companies; and the second,
abolishing EMPORCHI's monopoly by transferring responsibility for transfer and
storage of goods, except in special ceses, to the pr-vate sector, and by
replacing the individual ports into Limited Liability Authorities and EMPORCHI's
headquarters into a holding type National Ports Corporation. This second law also
authorized the private sector to participate both in the provision and operation
of ports. Except for the incorporation of the private sector, Law No. 18,042 was
never fully applied. Finally, Law No. 18,966 of 1990 abolished Law No. 18,042,
establishing that the sector would continue to be ruled by the original Law No.
290 (1960) modified, however, to exclude EMPORCHI from stevedoring and cargo
handling functions which would remain in private hands, except when required to
provide for temporary shortages.
4.        EMPORCHI is responsible for administrating eleven commercial public
ports along the Chilean coast, namely, Arica, Iquiqtue, Antofagasta, Coquimbo,
Valparaiso, San Antonio, Talcahuano and San Vicente ( the last two often
consxdered as one complex), Puerto Montt, Chacabuco and Punta Arenas. Although



- 77 -
ANNEX 6
Page 2 of 6
the performance of each port is measured separately, EMPORCHI exercises a very
tight control over the ports' financial management. The ports are required to
transfer daily their total revenues to headquarters, which in turn transfers
back, on agreed dates, amounts required for payment of salaries, suppliers and
other previously approved outflows. The ports cash surpluses are therefore
managed by EMPORCHI's main office. Regarding capital investments, even though
each port formulates annually its own investment program, it is reviewed and
revised as necessary by the main office taking into account the entity's long
term development plans as well as the Government's general investment framework.
5.        On  financial  management  aspects,  EMPORCHI  must  abide  by  the
regulations imposed by the Ministry of Finance   to all autonomous public
enterprises. Specifically, the Ministry of Finance is entitled to request from
the latter, by decree, the transfer of all cash surpluses generated  to the
national budget, up to a maximum of each entity's net income after tax. Finance
makes use of this privilege and EMPORCHI after payment of the income taxes it is
subject to, has annually been transferring its profits to the Government. Even
more, considering that the amount to be transferred is determined at the
beginning of each year during the budget approval process, and EMPORCHI is
required to make advanced monthly payments, there have been years (para 6) in
which, because of lower than expected financial results, actual transfers have
exceeded the entity's net income.
C. Cost and Tariff System
6.        In 1986-87, in order to improve its financial and management systems,
EMPORCHI assisted by the Catholic University of Chile, embarked in a series of
reforms including the introduction of a new chart of accounts, cost accounting
by port and service, and a decentralized budgeting system. The new costing system
was aimed at providing information to evaluate operational and financial
performance by center of responsibility and to provide the basis for the
development, on a second stage, of a new tariff structure based on the margiral
cost of services prov'ded. The tariff study followed, in 1988, and was carried
out also by the Catholic University of Chile with financing from Loan 2504-CH.
The study proposed a tariff structure based on two different charges for the use
of port facilities --wharfage and cargo transferred-- and a separate tariff
policy for the rest of the services provided. Charges on wharfage would aim at
alleviating congestion in the system through improved resource allocation, while
charges on cargo transferred would pursue cost recovery of port infrastructure.
The level of charges for the rest of the services would be based strictly upon
market considerations, ensuring, though, coverage of direct and economic costs.
However, complementary work is now required in order to put into practice the new
tariff structure recommended by the Cathol'c University. This task will be
undertaken by ME and MINTRATEL, with Bank assiL ance, under the proposed project
and financing for ouch purpose has beez. included in the technical assistance
component.



- 78 -
ANNEX 6
Page 3 of 6
D. Past Financial Performance
7.        EMPORCHI's actual income statements for the period 1982-1990 are
presented in Table 1 of this Annex. A summary follows:
Item                      1982       1984       1986      1988       1990
(In mid-1991 Chil $ billion)
Operating Revenues        16.1       14.'      18.5       19.3     19.5
Working costs              (6.3)     (5.6)      (6.3)     (6.8)    (6.7)
